### SA-1 — Policy and Procedures (Control)

This requirement is about making sure the organisation has clear, written rules for how it buys and sets up new systems and services, and that the right people know those rules and follow them. Without this, contracts and projects can lose direction, important legal and regulatory duties may be missed, and security and privacy weaknesses can be introduced simply because different teams take different approaches.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must create an organisation-wide policy for acquiring systems and services, along with supporting procedures. These must explain why the policy exists, what it covers, who is responsible for what, how senior leadership supports it, how different teams work together, and how compliance will be checked. The policy and procedures must also be consistent with all relevant laws, directives, regulations, and internal standards.

A named senior owner—either the Chief Information Security Officer (CISO) or a delegated Security Policy Manager—must be responsible for producing, documenting, and sharing the policy and procedures with security and privacy leadership, contracting officers, and programme managers. The procedures must also be shared with contracting and delivery staff and system security officers.

The policy and procedures must be reviewed at least every year. They must also be updated after audit findings, security incidents or breaches, major changes to system design, or changes in laws or directives. For the procedures, updates are also required after changes to acquisition processes, significant changes to vendors or contracting arrangements, or new or revised requirements.

##### CISO-owned acquisition security & privacy policy and procedures suite

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Draft “System and Services Acquisition Security & Privacy Policy” (authoritative suite) including purpose, scope, risk-management alignment, security/privacy coordination expectations, roles/responsibilities, and mapping of applicable laws/directives/regulations/standards to acquisition activities | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Conduct policy review workshops with Security, Privacy, Procurement/Contracting, and Legal/Compliance; incorporate feedback and produce approval-ready version | Project Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Establish policy governance and change-control mechanism (lightweight forum terms of reference, decision log/change ticket workflow, versioning approach, and traceability to defined triggers) | Cyber Security Lead | Enterprise | 12h | — |
| Draft “Acquisition Security & Privacy Procedures” with role-based workflows (contracting officers, acquisition programme staff, system security officers) including required inputs/outputs and evidence artefacts per acquisition lifecycle stage | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 28h | — |
| Define dissemination and evidence capture approach (controlled repository structure, distribution lists, acknowledgement/attendance capture method, and minimum evidence set for audits) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 10h | — |
| Execute dissemination and publish policy/procedures (upload to controlled repository, send communications, collect acknowledgements, and record publication/version history) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 12h | — |
| Implement annual review and event-trigger update process (cadence calendar, trigger definitions, responsibilities, review checklists, and documented rationale when no trigger occurs) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 14h | — |
| Produce audit-ready evidence pack template (review/approval minutes, change-control tickets, dissemination records, and version history) and validate completeness via tabletop exercise | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 10h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 126h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Draft the organisation-wide system and services procurement security and privacy policy, including its purpose, coverage (scope), how it links to risk management, and how it aligns with legal and regulatory requirements.
- [ ] Assign the Chief Information Security Officer (CISO) (or the delegated Security Policy Manager) as the official owner, and document their responsibilities for creating, approving, sharing, and reviewing the policy.
- [ ] Create role-based acquisition security and privacy procedures for contracting officers, acquisition programme staff, and system security officers, including the required inputs, outputs, and supporting evidence records.
- [ ] Publish both documents in a controlled document repository, and record when they were shared and when the defined policy and procedure recipients acknowledged receipt.
- [ ] Set up a documented review and update schedule: review the policy and procedures every year.
- [ ] Set up and run updates to the policy and procedures based on specific triggers:

- **Policy updates** when there are: audit findings, incidents or breaches, major changes to the system architecture, or changes to legal requirements or Department of Defense (DoD) directives.
- **Procedure updates** when there are: new or revised requirements for the SA family, changes to the acquisition workflow, major changes involving vendors or contracting, or updates to regulations.
- [ ] Set up a governance forum and a change-approval process to authorise updates and keep audit-ready records (meeting minutes, version history, and change request tickets).


---

### SA-2 — Allocation of Resources (Control)

This requirement is about making sure your organisation plans and pays for protection for every system and service from the start, not as an afterthought. If you do not identify security and privacy needs early and fund them properly, you risk not protecting sensitive information and personal data enough, not meeting your privacy obligations, and leaving weaknesses that could be exploited or cause harm—including through weaknesses in suppliers and other third parties.

To meet this expectation, during mission and business planning you must identify and record the main (high-level) information security and privacy requirements for each in-scope system or service. Then, through your capital planning and investment approval process, you must decide, document, and allocate the resources needed to meet those requirements across the full lifecycle—covering buying the system, keeping it running, and managing supply-chain risks. Finally, you must record information security and privacy funding as separate, clearly identifiable line items in your programme and budgeting documents.

##### Security & privacy requirements-to-budget traceability for each system

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Establish governance and approval gate criteria for requirements-to-budget traceability (SA-2 alignment), including sign-off roles and minimum evidence requirements | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Create/maintain authoritative system/service register fields required for traceability (owner, data categories, lifecycle stage, in-scope/out-of-scope flags) and publish operating instructions | Enterprise Architecture / Portfolio Management | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Standardise mission/business planning templates to capture high-level information security and high-level privacy requirements per system/service with evidence links (risk assessment, regulatory/contractual obligations, data classification) | Information Security & Privacy Governance Lead | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Develop control-to-cost mapping library translating requirement types into costed lifecycle resource categories (acquisition, sustainment, supply-chain risk) with definitions and cost-centre mapping rules | Information Security & Privacy Governance Lead | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Produce traceability pack generation pack (per capital bid) that outputs requirement → mapped resource categories → lifecycle phase → cost centre, including audit trail structure and document naming/versioning conventions | Project Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Implement discrete budget line item population rules in budgeting/programming artefacts (two separated sections/rows: Information Security and Privacy) and align with finance submission formats | Finance / Capital Planning Office | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Integrate traceability pack into capital planning and investment control workflow (approval gates enforce presence of security/privacy inputs, mapped resources, and discrete budget line items) and run pilot on 2–3 in-scope systems | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Train system/service owners and portfolio teams; perform pilot validation and audit-readiness review (evidence completeness, traceability accuracy, and budget line separation), then iterate templates/mapping rules | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 24h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 224h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create or update an official system and service register, including the named owner and the current lifecycle stage.
- [ ] Publish mission and business planning templates that capture high-level information security and privacy requirements, with links to supporting evidence.
- [ ] Develop and maintain a library that links each control to its cost, covering activities related to acquisition, ongoing support (sustainment), and supply-chain risk.
- [ ] Implement a traceability pack generator that links each requirement to the costed life-cycle resources used for each investment bid.
- [ ] Update the capital planning and investment submission form to require two separate budget line items: **Information Security** and **Privacy**.
- [ ] Add approval gate checks to stop approvals when the traceability pack evidence or individual line items are missing.


---

### SA-3 — System Development Life Cycle (Control)

This expectation is about making sure every system and service is built and changed in a controlled way, with clear checkpoints from the start to protect people’s data and privacy. Without this, organisations risk releasing products with avoidable weaknesses, handling personal information incorrectly, or missing and not managing risks early. This can lead to expensive rework, regulatory action, reputational damage, and harm to customers or staff.

The organisation must use its tailored system development approach, aligned with its risk management approach, and include security and privacy “gates” at the right points. This ensures these issues are considered during planning, building, testing, and rollout. It must also clearly define, document, and assign responsibility for security and responsibility for privacy across the entire development process, including naming the specific people in those roles. Finally, it must build its security risk management and privacy risk management processes into the development activities, including the checkpoints.

##### Tailored SDLC with security/privacy engineering gates and evidence

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Tailored SDLC + gate model definition: stage names, gate points, re-entry triggers, and mapping to “DoD RMF tailored SDLC with security and privacy engineering gates” (sa-03_odp) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Gate entry/exit criteria and required security/privacy artefacts: define minimum artefact set per gate (requirements, design, build, test/verification, release/deployment, operations/change, retirement) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 56h | — |
| Risk integration: define how gate exit criteria reference latest information security risk decisions and privacy risk decisions (residual risk sign-off, mitigations, acceptance records) and align with risk management strategy | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Workflow and approvals design: implement role-based gate approval model (authorised security/privacy roles), define evidence linkage requirements to system/service record and change/release records | Project Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| CI/CD automated gate checks (pipeline steps) specification and implementation for representative application: SBOM + vulnerability thresholds, secrets scanning, IaC/config baseline checks, mandatory security test evidence capture | DevOps Lead | Department | 64h | £6,000 |
| Evidence capture and retention integration: configure delivery toolchain to store/retain gate artefacts and automated scan/test outputs per release/change, including audit trail and retention tagging | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 32h | £2,500 |
| Supplier/developer/integrator extension: define contractual gate artefact requirements and integrate supplier evidence submission into the same gate workflow for at least one supplier delivery stream | Service Delivery Manager | Business Unit | 40h | £3,000 |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 288h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £11,500

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the specific software development life cycle (SDLC) steps and the security and privacy checkpoints that must be met, aligned with the organisation’s risk management strategy.
- [ ] Create entry and exit criteria for each stage of the lifecycle, and define the required security and privacy evidence checklists for each milestone.
- [ ] Add information security and privacy risk decision references to the criteria used when a project moves through its approval gates and to the approval workflow.
- [ ] Put in place controls that require every software change to automatically run security and privacy checks through the continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) process. This includes checks such as dependency and software bill of materials (SBOM) reviews, secrets scanning, configuration policy checks, and collecting the required test evidence.
- [ ] Set clear responsibility and approval authority for security and privacy roles, linking each role to the specific “gate” or decision point it controls (and name specific individuals where required).
- [ ] Set up a way to capture and keep evidence that connects the gate checks, scan and test results, and approvals to each system or service change record.
- [ ] Update supplier, developer, and integrator onboarding and contract requirements to provide the same gate deliverables and supporting evidence.


---

#### SA-3.1 — Manage Preproduction Environment (Enhancement)

This requirement is about protecting not only your live systems, but also your development, testing and integration workspaces. If these pre-production environments are not tightly controlled, attackers or accidental changes could go unnoticed. Harmful code could be tested and then mistakenly moved into later stages, and risky third-party software or supplier components could be added before anyone realises.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must first identify all pre-production environments. It must then apply protection measures that match the level of risk and potential impact if something goes wrong, and apply these controls consistently from early development through testing and integration.

The organisation must also set out, in documented development processes, how security and supplier-related risks are considered at each stage. This should be supported by plans and strategies, programme protection planning procedures, and the results of a criticality analysis.

Finally, it must clearly assign named responsibilities for both security and development activities, keep the supporting records, and demonstrate that protections are followed. Any exceptions must be formally approved and limited to a defined time period.

##### Risk-tiered preproduction environment baselines with automated drift checks

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---|---:|---:|
| Establish the preproduction environment register (including ephemeral CI/CD sandboxes) with required fields (ID/name, purpose, owning team, boundaries, data classification scope, lifecycle stage) and a documented intake/update process | Project Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Perform criticality analysis mapping for each registered environment to assign risk tiers (Tier 1/2/3) and produce an auditable mapping record linked to system/service criticality and data sensitivity | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Define tier-specific “golden baselines” (identity/access, secure configuration, patch/vulnerability posture, logging/monitoring, change control) and publish them as version-controlled baseline specifications | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 48h | — |
| Implement policy-as-code and configuration compliance checks (IaC policy checks + CSPM/CM rules where applicable) aligned to the golden baselines, including rule testing and false-positive tuning | Platform Engineer | Enterprise | 72h | £18,000 |
| Integrate CI/CD stage gates for dev→test→integration promotions with automated compliance evaluation, signed artefact enforcement (where feasible), and clear failure/override workflow | DevOps Lead | Enterprise | 56h | £6,000 |
| Build drift detection and remediation workflow (scheduled + event-driven scans, ticket automation, re-validation requirements before integration use, and closure evidence capture) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 64h | £10,000 |
| Implement audit evidence retention (compliance reports/snapshots, pipeline attestations, access logs, exception approvals) into an auditable repository with retention alignment and access controls | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 32h | £4,000 |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 328h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £38,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep an up-to-date register of pre-production environments, covering development, testing, integration, and temporary continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) sandboxes.
- [ ] Assign each pre-production environment to a risk level based on the results of the recorded criticality analysis.
- [ ] Set “golden” baseline standards for each tier covering identity and access, secure system settings, patching and vulnerability status, and logging and monitoring.
- [ ] Set up “policy-as-code” and continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) stage gates so that releases cannot be promoted if the baseline compliance checks fail.
- [ ] Set up continuous compliance checks and automated fixes for any configuration changes (drift) in each environment tier.
- [ ] Set up how long audit records are kept for compliance reports, pipeline attestations, and any time-limited exceptions.


---

#### SA-3.2 — Use of Live or Operational Data (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that when teams use real, live information while building, testing, or integrating systems, that use is formally approved, properly recorded, and strictly limited. Without this, sensitive customer or business information could be exposed, copied, misused, or accessed by the wrong people during testing—causing real harm even before the system goes live.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:

- approve in advance which pre-production environments are allowed to use live (operational) data for a specific system or service  
- document the purpose and scope of that approved use  
- keep access tightly restricted so only the approved use happens  

The organisation must also protect these pre-production environments to the same level of sensitivity and risk as the live data they contain. Finally, it must keep evidence that access is authorised, recorded (logged), and removed when no longer needed, with ongoing review to ensure approvals and protections stay up to date.

##### Live data permits and policy-as-code gates for pre-production

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Design the Live/Operational Data Permit workflow (ticket fields, approval routing, required metadata, expiry handling, and audit evidence requirements) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Define security baseline controls per data classification and document “pre-production baseline ≥ production” equivalence requirements (encryption, logging/monitoring, retention, network access, access model) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Implement permit registry and API/DB model (store permit scope: environments, dataset/field identifiers, purpose, PII indicator, risk acceptance reference, compensating controls, expiry) | Platform Engineer | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Develop policy-as-code enforcement for permit validation and allowlisting (dataset/field scope, permitted environments, approved pipelines/ETL jobs, identity/role constraints) | Software Lead | Enterprise | 56h | £18,000 |
| Restrict “no ad-hoc live data movement” by enforcing approved transfer mechanisms only (credential scoping, pipeline whitelisting, deny-by-default for direct loads) | DevOps Lead | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Implement audit logging, monitoring, and alerting (permit validation events, transfer job execution, loaded identifiers, access to pre-production live-data stores; SIEM integration and alert rules) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 32h | £6,000 |
| Automate expiry and removal (revocation of access, deletion/anonymisation workflows for approved scope, and retention of audit logs) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 24h | — |

Total Estimated Effort: **270h**  
Total Estimated Cost: **£24,000**

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a “Live/Operational Data Permit” template that records: the environment, the dataset and field scope, the purpose, the data classification and whether it includes personal data (PII), any approved risk acceptance, and the expiry date.
- [ ] Set up a controlled approval process that includes sign-off from Information Security and Privacy where it applies.
- [ ] Create “policy-as-code” allowlists that only permit transfers to approved pre-production environments, approved datasets and fields, and approved transfer pipelines.
- [ ] Restrict live data loading to pipelines that have been approved by the permit process, by removing or limiting any one-off export/import credentials and any direct tooling paths.
- [ ] Set up security standards for the pre-production environment (encryption, logging and monitoring, data retention, and network and access restrictions) to be at least as strong as those used in the production environment for the same data classification.
- [ ] Set up security information and event management (SIEM) audit logs for permit checks, transfer execution, and access to pre-production data. Add alerts for transfers made without a permit or for unauthorised access.
- [ ] Automate the expiry of permits so that access is removed and the approved live-data scope is deleted or anonymised, while keeping audit evidence for record-keeping.


---

#### SA-3.3 — Technology Refresh (Enhancement)

This expectation is about keeping the organisation’s technology up to date on purpose—not by accident. You must create and follow a planned schedule for updating the technology throughout its whole life. This includes the hardware, software, and any embedded software, as well as the supporting ways of working, the skills of the people who run it, the suppliers or service providers you rely on, and the locations where updates are carried out.

Without this, the organisation may end up using equipment or software that no longer receives support or updates. That can leave known weaknesses unpatched, increase the risk of unreliable or incorrectly sourced parts, and make it harder to meet privacy and security responsibilities—leading to service disruption, compliance failures, and avoidable errors.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must:
- set clear timing for refresh activities from planning through operations and maintenance
- build this planning into how systems are bought and built
- base refresh decisions on documented security and privacy risk assessments
- record the schedule and the evidence that refresh actions were completed as planned
- keep the relevant plans and responsibilities up to date

##### Risk-based technology refresh schedule with SDLC gate evidence

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Establish authoritative technology inventory template and data model (incl. hardware/software/embedded firmware; fields for version, environment, owner, supplier, support status, security update availability, and evidence links) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Populate and validate technology inventory for the application and dependencies (dev/test/prod/DR), including supplier/service provider identification and support-status verification | Infrastructure Lead | Enterprise | 56h | — |
| Define risk-based refresh triggers and timing rules (e.g., vendor end-of-security-updates windows, immediate review triggers, control-miss triggers) and publish decision criteria | Cyber Security Lead | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Perform security and privacy risk assessments for refresh decisions and create an approval record structure linking each scheduled item to the relevant risk assessment outputs | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Create controlled technology refresh schedule as an auditable backlog (stage-gated items with target action, affected components, due date/trigger, maintenance window, testing/validation expectations, and linked risk references) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Implement SDLC gate evidence requirements (design/build obsolescence check; production refresh-readiness verification) and update change/release templates to require rollback plan and completion evidence (inventory/config updates, release notes, test results) | Project Manager | Enterprise | 48h | — |
| Extend refresh planning to supporting capabilities (update operational runbooks, patch/upgrade procedures, and competency/training requirements; ensure supplier/service provider contractual update obligations are captured and tracked) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 24h | £8,000 |
| Establish audit-ready evidence retention and ongoing schedule maintenance process (evidence index, periodic review cadence, and change notifications when component support status changes) | Service Delivery Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 280h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £8,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an official inventory of each application and its dependency components, including their version or firmware, the environment they run in, the responsible owner, the supplier, and whether vendor support is available.
- [ ] Define documented times when updates must be refreshed (for example, when security updates reach a set threshold) and specify the required maintenance windows.
- [ ] Create a security and privacy risk assessment template to support refresh decisions, and require a risk reference for each item being refreshed.
- [ ] Publish a controlled technology refresh plan that covers upgrades, replacements, and retirement (decommissioning) across the full life cycle.
- [ ] Add obsolescence and “ready for refresh” checks to the software development life cycle (SDLC) approval checkpoints, and require proof before any release to production.
- [ ] Carry out each refresh using approved change records, including a rollback plan, proof of testing, and updated inventory and configuration records.
- [ ] Keep audit-ready records that link each scheduled item to its risk assessment results and the evidence showing it was completed, and keep the schedule up to date.


---

### SA-4 — Acquisition Process (Control)

This requirement is about making sure that when you buy or commission a system or service, the contract clearly states what the supplier must do to protect your information and people’s privacy. Without this, a supplier could provide something that looks acceptable on paper but is weaker than expected, does not include the right protections, cannot show what was built and tested, or provides incomplete or poorly protected documentation—making it harder for you to operate safely, investigate problems, or hold anyone accountable.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must use its SA-4 acquisition template to set out in the contract (or by referring to version-controlled documents) the security and privacy requirements, the required strength of the protective measures, the evidence the supplier must provide from development and testing, the specific safeguards the supplier must put in place, and the security and privacy documents the supplier must produce and protect. The contract must also describe the supplier’s build and test environment and the intended operating environment, clearly assign responsibilities for security, privacy, and supply-chain risk, and set measurable acceptance criteria to decide whether the work is truly acceptable.

##### SA-4 contract annex pack with security/privacy traceability

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---|---:|---:|
| Establish and publish a version-controlled “SA-4 Annex Pack” template aligned to {{ insert: param, sa-04_odp.01 }} (clause sections for functional requirements, strength of mechanism, assurance/evidence, documentation deliverables & protection, environments, responsibility allocation, acceptance criteria) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Define the traceability model and mapping rules (requirements → evidence deliverables → acceptance tests/sign-off) including required identifiers/fields to support audit-ready verification | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Run SA-2-to-SA-4 requirements derivation workshop(s) for procurement scopes (facilitate mapping to concrete, testable functional requirements and measurable acceptance criteria) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Populate strength-of-mechanism and assurance requirements in the Annex Pack for typical acquisition categories (e.g., authentication/authorisation, encryption, logging/monitoring, secure configuration, tamper/bypass resistance) with capability-specific targets | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Define supplier evidence deliverables and evidence handling requirements (development artefacts, assessment/test reports, vulnerability evidence, documentation protection requirements, secure transfer/distribution constraints) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Create contract insertion/reference guidance for Procurement/Legal (how to insert {{ insert: param, sa-04_odp.01 }}, when to reference annex pack sections, and how to handle deviations/exception approvals) | Project Manager | Enterprise | 12h | — |
| Define and document acceptance testing and sign-off workflow (evidence review steps, acceptance test outcomes, measurable criteria, and responsibility for verification) | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 14h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 126h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a version-controlled “SA-4 Annex Pack” that follows the organisation’s SA-4 acquisition template wording ({{ insert: param, sa-04_odp.01 }})
- [ ] Update the procurement process so that, for every in-scope purchase, the SA-4 Annex Pack is either inserted or referenced.
- [ ] Run a Security Assessment 2 to Security Assessment 4 (SA-2 to SA-4) derivation workshop, as specified in the procurement process, to produce security and privacy functional requirements that can be tested, along with the acceptance criteria.
- [ ] Set strength targets and expected resistance levels for each specific mechanism included in the acquired scope.
- [ ] Specify what supplier assurance evidence must be provided (development and assessment records, plus test and verification reports) and link each item to the acceptance criteria.
- [ ] Add lifecycle documentation deliverables and contractual clauses that require those documentation assets to be protected during transfer, storage, and distribution.
- [ ] Define clear, measurable acceptance tests and set out how evidence will be reviewed and formally approved in the contract, so acceptance decisions can be made objectively.


---

#### SA-4.1 — Functional Properties of Controls (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people who build or supply your system clearly explain, in plain language, how the security and privacy features will work for users. This matters because if you only receive vague promises or technical detail, you may not understand what actually happens when people try to use the system. That can result in weaker protection, privacy problems, or unexpected findings during audits and day-to-day operations.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must require the developer or supplier to provide a written description of the security and privacy features that users can see or experience at the points where they interact with the system. The description should focus on what users can see or do and what results they will get. It should not include hidden internal workings or details about internal data.

This requirement must be included in your procurement process. It should appear in invitations to bid, procurement documents, and contracts. It should also be reflected in your system security and privacy planning documents.

##### Procurement deliverable for interface-visible security/privacy control behaviour

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---|---:|---:|
| Draft the “Interface Behaviour & Functional Properties Description” template (per in-scope control) including required fields, exclusions (no internal implementation/data structures), and example entries | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Define the control-to-interface mapping guidance (how suppliers should identify UI/API/CLI/SSO/workflow points; how to express observable outcomes, inputs/outputs, success/denial behaviour, and role/permission differences) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Update procurement/sales solicitation documents and contract clauses to make the deliverable a contractual obligation (submission timing, update-on-material-change requirement, and acceptance gate linkage) | Project Manager | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Establish the supplier submission and document control workflow (intake, versioning, traceability to contract control lists, and storage location for audit readiness) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 14h | — |
| Configure the acceptance gate process for security/privacy evidence (review checklist, completeness criteria, traceability rules, and escalation path for non-conforming submissions) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 18h | — |
| Run a pilot with 1–2 active suppliers/projects to validate the template and acceptance gate; capture gaps and revise template/guidance accordingly | Programme/Project manager | Business Unit | 20h | — |
| Deliver training/enablement pack for procurement and security/privacy SMEs (how to review for interface-visible behaviour, how to reject internal implementation disclosures, and how to document approvals) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 12h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 124h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an acquisition deliverable template that requires interface-visible functional features for each specified security and privacy control.
- [ ] Add a contract clause requiring suppliers to provide the template, and to exclude any internal implementation details and internal data structures.
- [ ] Update the request documents (request for proposal (RFP) / invitation to tender (ITT)) to require submission of the template at a specific point in the project lifecycle and for any material changes.
- [ ] Set up a procurement approval “gate” that stops contracts being finalised or accepted unless the required deliverable is available and has been linked to the relevant control list.
- [ ] Create a security and privacy subject-matter expert (SME) review checklist to check what users can see in the interface, whether requests succeed or are blocked, and whether results differ by role (role-based access control (RBAC)).
- [ ] Log approved deliverables in the system security and privacy planning evidence repository so they can be traced during audits.


---

#### SA-4.2 — Design and Implementation Information for Controls (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that anyone who builds or supplies a system gives you clear, complete paperwork describing how the security features are designed and assembled. This lets you check that the security has been built correctly and will work as intended. Without this, you could end up buying something that appears secure at first glance, but has weaknesses, hidden gaps, or parts that do not fit together properly. You would then struggle to prove what you actually received and to test it properly before relying on it.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must include these documentation needs in procurement from the start. Your contracts and related buying documents should require the supplier to provide:

- a high-level design: how the security-relevant parts fit together and connect to other systems or users  
- a low-level design: how the individual components are built and how they connect internally  

The information should be detailed enough for your team to analyse, test, and verify.

Your organisation should also define in advance what “sufficient detail” means, make sure this requirement is used when awarding contracts, and keep records showing that the requirement was included and that the information delivered can be traced to what you will check.

##### Security Design Pack deliverables for SA-4(2) procurement

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---|---:|---:|
| Draft Security Design Pack procurement clause (SA-4(2) aligned) including required content: high-level design, low-level design, implementation representation, supplier/product identification and integrity artefacts | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |

| Define “sufficient detail” standard for acceptance (what constitutes analysable/testable/verifiable detail; minimum granularity for interfaces/modules; required traceability expectations) | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Define contract acceptance criteria and rejection workflow (mapping of each security-relevant control requirement to specific HLD/LLD sections; interface description requirements at both levels; traceability to delivered build artefacts/schematics) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Implement procurement gating in contracting workflow (template enforcement, mandatory deliverables schedule attachment, pre-sign-off checklist, and escalation path for incomplete packs) | Service Delivery Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Update internal guidance for system/solution owners on how to request and review packs (review checklist, evidence expectations, and how to validate independent review and test derivation) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 12h | — |
| Pilot the Security Design Pack on 1–2 representative procurements and refine templates/acceptance criteria based on findings | Project Manager | Business Unit | 24h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 132h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Draft a standard procurement clause that requires a “Security Design Pack” for security-relevant controls.
- [ ] Define and publish the organisation’s standard for what counts as “enough information” to support analysis, testing, and verification.
- [ ] Specify that the delivery schedule must include mandatory high-level design information, including security-relevant subsystems and the interfaces between those subsystems.
- [ ] Specify that the delivery schedule must include mandatory low-level design details, including security-relevant components and how those components connect through defined interfaces.
- [ ] Add acceptance criteria that require traceability from each security-relevant control to the relevant design sections and the verification evidence.
- [ ] Where relevant, require references to the implementation being described (for example, source code or build artefact identifiers, or schematic references).
- [ ] Set up procurement approval checks so contracts cannot be finalised unless the delivery schedule and the acceptance criteria are included.


---

#### SA-4.3 — Development Methods, Techniques, and Practices (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people who build or supply your system can clearly show how they plan, build, test, and approve it. This helps prevent hidden mistakes from slipping through. Without this, problems in the design or code could go unnoticed until after launch, leaving weaknesses that attackers could exploit, or privacy issues that are harder to fix later.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must require the developer to follow a complete, documented development process. This process must include model-based planning so that every requirement can be traced to the work done and the tests that prove it. It must also keep controlled records of what was built and how it changed.

The developer must also follow the specific secure engineering methods your organisation requires, including:
- thinking about threats (how things could go wrong and be attacked),
- security design reviews, and
- linking security needs to the tests that confirm they are met.

They must also follow the privacy engineering methods your organisation requires, including:
- data minimisation,
- privacy threat thinking, and
- privacy impact assessments.

They must follow the secure software development methods your organisation requires, including:
- checking code,
- running tests where appropriate, and
- peer code review.

They must verify the system through unit testing, integration testing, system testing, and security testing, with coverage measures.

Finally, they must carry out quality checks using clear acceptance rules, peer reviews, defect tracking, and formal release readiness gates. They must provide evidence through acquisition documents and contracts.

##### Evidence-first SDLC with MBSE traceability and security/privacy artefacts

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define the Evidence-First Life Cycle Evidence Pack (LCEP) template (IDs, versioning, scope boundaries, approver roles, required evidence items, and traceability link rules) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Create procurement and delivery acceptance requirements for LCEP submission (contract clauses, acceptance criteria, evidence format requirements, and audit/retention expectations) | Project Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Establish MBSE traceability evidence requirements (require model/repository exports showing requirement ID → model/design elements → test case IDs → test results; define baseline and change-history expectations) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Establish security engineering evidence requirements (threat modelling outputs, security architecture review records, security requirements traceability to design and tests; define minimum review/approval evidence) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Establish privacy engineering evidence requirements (privacy threat modelling and privacy impact assessment outcomes where applicable; define linkage to requirements/design/tests and evidence retention) | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Define SSDLC evidence requirements (static analysis, dynamic/runtime testing where applicable, peer code review sign-offs; require defect/issue tracking with closure status and linkage to affected requirements/tests) | Software Lead | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Define verification/validation and quality control evidence requirements (coverage metrics across unit/integration/system/security testing; handling of coverage gaps; acceptance criteria, defect tracking, peer reviews, and release readiness gate records) | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 24h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 144h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an evidence pack template for an evidence-first life cycle (LCEP), mapped to the organisation’s chosen methods: model-based systems engineering (MBSE) traceability, security and privacy engineering, secure software development life cycle (SSDLC), verification and validation (V&V), and quality control.
- [ ] Add contract clauses requiring the developer to provide auditable LCEP evidence and end-to-end traceability exports (from requirements to design/model to tests to results), using version-controlled baseline records.
- [ ] Require security engineering evidence (threat models, security architecture review records, and security requirements traceability) to be linked to the same traceability graph.
- [ ] Require privacy engineering evidence—privacy threat modelling and privacy impact assessments—to be produced and linked to the requirements, design, and tests for any privacy methods that are selected or required.
- [ ] Require software development lifecycle (SDLC) evidence, including static code analysis, live testing during operation where applicable, and peer code review sign-offs. Track each finding to completion (closure).
- [ ] Require verification and validation (V&V) reporting that includes coverage metrics for unit, integration, system, and security testing, and documented plans for how any coverage gaps are handled.
- [ ] Set clear “release readiness” approval requirements, including what must be met for acceptance, how defects are tracked, how peer reviews are completed, and what “closed” means. Make these requirements mandatory for final delivery sign-off.


---

#### SA-4.5 — System, Component, and Service Configurations (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring systems are built and re-built using the same safe, pre-approved security settings every time. Without this, a new system, software update, or reinstallation could quietly use weaker or risky default settings—for example, unnecessary features left enabled, open access points, or configurations that make it easier for criminals to break in or cause harm.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the system or service developer to deliver the platform using the organisation’s specified “hardened” security configuration baselines. This includes applying the United States Government Configuration Baseline (USGCB) and the Department of Defense Security Technical Implementation Guide (DoD STIG) settings for the platform, turning off any unused services and ports, and applying hardened secure configuration options.

The organisation must also ensure that if the system is reinstalled or upgraded later, the same baselines are used as the default configuration, rather than being replaced with weaker defaults.

##### Baseline-as-a-deliverable for USGCB/DoD STIG secure configs

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define the per-platform “Baseline Pack” template (scope, versioning, USGCB/DoD STIG mapping, configuration item structure, unused services/ports allow-deny list format) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Produce Baseline Packs for each in-scope platform component (OS, middleware/runtime, container base images, network services) including explicit STIG/USGCB versions and hardened configuration characteristics | Architect | Enterprise | 56h | — |
| Update acquisition artefacts (RFQs/solicitations and contract/SOW clauses) to require: Baseline Pack implementation, deny-list enforcement, delivery of reinstall/upgrade artefacts, and audit-ready evidence at handover | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Implement baseline-aligned reinstall/upgrade artefacts (golden VM/container images, IaC modules, configuration management profiles, deployment pipeline templates) with reproducible build/deploy mechanisms | Platform Engineer | Business Unit | 80h | £6,000 |
| Define and run acceptance evidence generation (STIG/USGCB-aligned compliance assessment report output + checklist mapping each baseline requirement to evidence, including explicit proof denied ports/services are not listening) | Security Engineer | Department | 48h | — |
| Establish controlled repository and traceability workflow (store Baseline Packs, evidence, and delivered artefacts; link to system security plan and change records; prevent drift via version control and release gating) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 32h | £2,500 |
| Handover and operational readiness (developer walkthrough, evidence pack packaging, and operational runbook updates for default baseline application during reinstall/upgrade) | Service Delivery Manager | Department | 24h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 288h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £8,500

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] For each platform, define an approved “Baseline Pack” that matches the exact United States Government Configuration Baseline (USGCB) and Department of Defense Security Technical Implementation Guide (DoD STIG) versions, and include a deny list for unused services and ports.
- [ ] Update requests for quotation (RFQs), statements of work (SOWs), and contracts to require developers to implement the Baseline Pack and to deliver reinstall and upgrade files that match the baseline requirements.
- [ ] Specify the evidence required to accept the system as compliant (a compliance report aligned to the Security Technical Implementation Guide (STIG) and United States Government Configuration Baseline (USGCB), plus a mapping checklist), including proof that any denied services or ports are not accepting connections.
- [ ] Require delivery of repeatable “golden” artefacts (hardened system images and/or infrastructure as code (IaC) and configuration profiles) that the organisation’s deployment pipeline uses as the default.
- [ ] Store the baseline pack, acceptance evidence, and delivered artefacts in a controlled repository, with traceability to the system security plan.
- [ ] Check the acceptance evidence during handover. If any required baseline item is missing, or if any blocked service or port is still present, reject the delivery.


---

#### SA-4.6 — Use of Information Assurance Products (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the tools used to protect sensitive, classified information are the correct, pre-approved ones, and that they have been properly checked before they are used. If an organisation uses the wrong protection products, or uses them in the wrong situation—for example, when the communication network is not classified at a lower level than the information—the protection may not work, and classified data could be exposed or handled incorrectly.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must use only standard, off-the-shelf protection products that, together, form an approved solution for this specific “lower-to-higher classification” communication scenario. Before deploying that solution, the organisation must confirm that each product has been assessed or tested by the relevant authority, or through approved procedures.

The organisation must also:
- keep an up-to-date list of what is currently deployed
- ensure purchasing and contracts only select products that match the approved solution and include the required proof of assessment
- record the details in its system planning documents
- assign clear responsibility for checking approvals and keeping the records

##### Enforce NSA-approved IA product eligibility via controlled catalogue

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define the authoritative IA product/solution catalogue schema (fields for vendor, exact version, configuration profile, scenario mapping, evidence links, validity dates, exception references) and publish governance for updates | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Build the controlled catalogue repository (version-controlled, access-controlled) and implement scenario-to-product mapping with validation rules (including identifier normalisation strategy) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Establish evaluation/validation evidence ingestion workflow (capture evaluation/validation record IDs, scope, dates, approval/procedure basis) and implement evidence linkage to exact product versions | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Integrate asset inventory sources (SBOMs, package manifests, container image digests, appliance firmware versions) and implement normalisation/matching to catalogue entries with traceable mapping logs | Platform Engineer | Enterprise | 56h | £18,000 |
| Implement policy-as-code eligibility gates in CI/CD and change/release workflows (block release when IA/IA-enabled cryptographic components are missing or lack evidence linkage for the required scenario) | DevOps Lead | Enterprise | 64h | £12,000 |
| Implement re-validation triggers for change control (version/configuration changes, crypto library updates, key-management parameter changes) and ensure automated re-checks with escalation to delegated authority when coverage/evidence no longer matches | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Produce audit-ready release evidence outputs (per system release report: IA components, catalogue mapping, linked evidence references; exportable for auditors) and run end-to-end pilot with at least one representative application | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 32h | — |

Total Estimated Effort: **288h**  
Total Estimated Cost: **£30,000**

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the communication situations, from lower to higher sensitivity, that require National Security Agency (NSA) approval for key management coverage.
- [ ] Build and maintain an authoritative information architecture (IA) product and solution catalogue that records the exact vendor and version for each item, and maps each item to the relevant scenarios.
- [ ] Link each catalogue entry to the evidence used for evaluation and validation, and record the validity dates and the approval or procedure basis.
- [ ] Link the inventory that is already deployed (software bill of materials (SBOM), software inventory, and firmware versions) to the catalogue using standardised identifiers.
- [ ] Set up “policy-as-code” release checkpoints that stop deployments if the information assurance (IA) components or versions do not have catalogue coverage or supporting evidence.
- [ ] Set up change-management workflows so that any update to an IA component’s version or configuration must be re-checked (re-validated) before it is released.
- [ ] For each release, produce audit evidence reports that link the deployed artificial intelligence (AI) components to the coverage approved by the National Security Agency (NSA) and to the relevant evaluation records.

#### SA-4.7 — NIAP-approved Protection Profiles  (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the security tools and security-enabled technology you buy are genuinely trustworthy before you rely on them. Without this check, a company could unknowingly install products with weak or unproven protections, which could leave customer data, business systems, or financial processes exposed to theft, disruption, or fraud.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set clear purchasing rules so that any commercially provided security product is only selected and used if it has been independently tested and approved against the relevant National Information Assurance Partnership (NIAP)-approved Protection Profile for its type of technology, where such an approved profile exists.

If no NIAP-approved Protection Profile exists for that type of technology, the organisation must only use the product if any built-in encryption used to enforce its security rules comes from a cryptographic module that is validated as Federal Information Processing Standards (FIPS)-approved or approved by the United States National Security Agency (NSA).

The organisation must build these checks into the procurement process, include the required proof in supplier requests and contracts, keep records of what was approved and why, maintain an up-to-date list of products in use, and assign named staff to verify compliance and ensure the correct evidence is kept.

##### Procurement assurance gate for NIAP PP or FIPS/NSA crypto validation

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define the Product Assurance gate decision rules (technology-type classification, NIAP PP existence check, evidence requirements, mismatch handling, and PO-block criteria) and publish as an auditable procurement standard | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Create and govern the technology-type mapping catalogue (technology type → applicable NIAP Protection Profile, or “none exists” rationale) including an escalation path for ambiguous classifications | Security Requirements Owner | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Design evidence data model and repository structure (supplier evidence metadata, versioning, linkage to product/inventory record, NIAP PP reference or FIPS/NSA cryptographic module reference, audit retention requirements) | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Configure procurement workflow integration to enforce the gate (mandatory fields, evidence upload/validation steps, PO release blocking logic, and workflow ownership/role enforcement) | Platform Engineer | Enterprise | 40h | £12,000 |
| Implement evidence verification workflow (Evidence Verifier review checklist, automated validation rules where possible, exception handling, and audit trail generation) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 24h | £6,000 |
| Build audit-ready reporting and evidence traceability (exportable audit packs per purchase, inventory linkage, and periodic compliance status dashboards) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 16h | £4,000 |
| Run pilot on a representative set of security-enabled purchases and remediate gaps (test cases for NIAP PP present vs absent, evidence mismatch scenarios, and bypass prevention validation) | Project Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Deliver training and operating procedures (role-based training for Security Requirements Owner, Evidence Verifier, and Procurement Compliance Approver; SOPs for suppliers and internal teams) | Service Delivery Manager | Enterprise | 12h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 172h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £22,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish the decision rule for choosing between the NIAP Protection Profile and FIPS/NSA cryptographic module evidence for Product Assurance.
- [ ] Create and keep up to date a catalogue that maps each technology type to the National Information Assurance Partnership (NIAP) Protection Profile, with an escalation route for cases where the classification is unclear.
- [ ] Set up the procurement process so that staff must upload supporting evidence, and so that purchase order (PO) creation is blocked if the evidence is missing or does not match the request.
- [ ] Set up an evidence repository, and link each approved product and version to its official inventory record, including the specific National Information Assurance Partnership (NIAP) protection profile or the Federal Information Processing Standards (FIPS) / National Security Agency (NSA) module reference.
- [ ] Assign named responsibilities (Security Requirements Owner, Evidence Verifier, Procurement Compliance Approver) and ensure the approval process is followed without any ability to skip steps.
- [ ] Run a trial on a clearly defined set of product categories that include security features, and check that audit records can be traced end to end.

#### SA-4.8 — Continuous Monitoring Plan for Controls (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that when a supplier builds a system or service, they also explain how they will keep its protective measures working properly over time. Without this, changes to the system, new threats, or gradual weaknesses could mean the protections stop working as intended, leaving the organisation exposed to unauthorised access, data loss, or service disruption.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must ask the supplier to provide a continuous monitoring plan that fits with the organisation’s own approach to ongoing checks. The plan should clearly set out what will be monitored and how often, how the supplier will judge whether the protections are still effective as things change, and what actions they will take if any protection fails or becomes ineffective, including how issues will be handled and escalated back to the organisation.

##### Developer continuous monitoring plan deliverable mapped to programme

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---|---:|---:|
| Define and publish the standard “Developer Continuous Monitoring Plan for Control Effectiveness” template (including required sections: control coverage mapping, monitoring activity catalogue, frequency matrix with triggers, effectiveness criteria, failure/remediation actions, evidence artefacts, and alignment statement) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Update procurement and contracting artefacts (SOW/contract/SLA) to add the mandatory deliverable, submission timing (pre-acceptance/onboarding), update triggers (material change + annual), and acceptance criteria wording | Project Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Create the plan content-check checklist and review workflow (GRC review steps, pass/fail criteria, escalation for non-conformance, and evidence-sharing expectations) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 12h | — |
| Establish onboarding operational mapping: create checklist items linking each plan element to organisational monitoring workflows (ticketing/escalation paths, evidence ingestion cadence, and reporting routes) | Security Governance Lead | Enterprise | 14h | — |
| Pilot the approach with 1–2 representative applications/suppliers: run template completion, perform content checks, validate control-to-programme mapping, and refine the template/checklist based on findings | Project Manager | Business Unit | 20h | — |
| Train procurement, GRC reviewers, and system/application owners on how to request, review, and accept the plan (including common failure modes and how to verify auditability) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 10h | — |
| Produce final guidance pack and governance artefacts: publish “how to comply” instructions, version control approach for annual/material-change updates, and retention expectations for evidence artefacts | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 10h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 106h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update the procurement templates to require a developer to provide a “Continuous Monitoring Plan for Control Effectiveness” deliverable.
- [ ] Create and publish a standard plan template that requires: a link between controls and programmes, the types of monitoring activities, a schedule showing how often each activity is carried out, and clear criteria for judging whether the monitoring is effective.
- [ ] Add contract clauses requiring updates to the plan when there are material changes, and at least once every year.
- [ ] Create and apply a review checklist to confirm that work matches the organisation’s continuous monitoring programme and that the correct escalation routes are followed.
- [ ] Require suppliers to provide evidence documents (“evidence artefacts”) and to state how often and by what method they will send them to the organisation.
- [ ] Create an onboarding integration checklist that connects supplier evidence to internal monitoring activities and to ticketing and escalation processes.


---

#### SA-4.9 — Functions, Ports, Protocols, and Services in Use (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people building or supplying a system clearly explain what it is meant to do and how it connects to other services. This helps the organisation understand exactly what it is buying and running. Without this, the system could include hidden or unnecessary features, open the wrong communication entry points, use the wrong agreed data-sharing rules, or provide capabilities the business never intended—leading to avoidable risk of misuse, disruption, or compliance problems.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the developer to document, in plain language, the specific functions; the network entry points (ports); the communication rules (protocols); and the services or capabilities intended for organisational use, including any external services the system relies on.

The organisation should also check that what is described in the system documentation and procurement documents matches what was agreed in contracts, requirements, and service agreements, with no contradictions unless the organisation has explicitly approved any differences.

|---|---|---:|---:|---:|

| Create the mapping-matrix validation rules (referential integrity, no blank mappings, protocol→function/service, service→ports/endpoints, external services explicitly identified) and document them as acceptance criteria | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 12h | — |
| Update governance: embed “Intended Use” pack as a procurement/SOW deliverable and add release-gate checklist items for developer completion and evidence retention | Project Manager | Enterprise | 10h | — |
| Implement pipeline/quality checks for pack completeness (e.g., schema/structure checks, mandatory field presence, mapping consistency) integrated into the delivery workflow | DevOps Lead | Enterprise | 24h | £8,000 |
| Perform security/consistency review guidance: publish a lightweight review procedure for Security Engineering to assess unambiguity and drift against available artefacts (e.g., manifests/API gateway/firewall rules) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 14h | — |
| Pilot the pack with 1–2 representative applications and refine template/checks based on findings; produce final “approved for enterprise use” version | Platform Engineer | Business Unit | 20h | — |
| Roll out training and operating model: run developer workshops, create FAQs, and establish ongoing refresh cadence (requirements/design stage + release refresh) | Service Delivery Manager | Enterprise | 12h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 108h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £8,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an organisation-controlled “Intended Use” template with sections for **Functions**, **Services**, **Protocols**, **Ports/Endpoints**, and a **required mapping matrix**.
- [ ] Update the procurement documents and statement of work (SOW), and the release acceptance criteria, to require the developer to submit the versioned “Intended Use” pack at the requirements and design stage and again with every release.
- [ ] Set up automated checks to confirm the information is complete and correctly linked—for example, every protocol is connected to a function or service, and every service lists its ports and endpoints.
- [ ] Set up a security architecture review process to confirm that every system mapping is clear and that any external or third-party services are clearly identified.
- [ ] Store the completed packs in a controlled repository and link each pack version to the relevant change record or release so it can be traced for audit purposes.
