#### SA-8.1 — Clear Abstractions (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure systems have simple, clearly defined ways for different parts to work together, so everyone knows exactly what each part does with information. Without this, people may accidentally use the system in the wrong way, internal details could affect how other parts behave, and unclear or repeated ways of doing the same task could cause mistakes that expose data or harm privacy.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must create and use written procedures that explain how to apply clear, well-defined ways for components to connect and work when specifying, designing, building, implementing, and changing systems and services. It must record the design decisions, include this approach in security and privacy requirements, architecture, and plans, and keep evidence showing that the approach was followed.

Named roles must be assigned to set these requirements, apply the procedures, ensure developers follow them, and oversee correct use. The connections between components must be necessary, sufficient, consistent, easy to understand, precise, and straightforward to review and test.

##### Contract-driven interfaces with information hiding and contract tests

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---|---:|---:|
| Establish contract catalogue governance (standards, naming/versioning rules, ownership model, change/deprecation workflow) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Inventory all externally callable interfaces and create versioned contract catalogue entries (including interface owner, boundary type, and contract artefact link) | Project Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Define interface contract artefacts for each interface using standard schema/IDL tooling (OpenAPI/AsyncAPI/protobuf/GraphQL schema as applicable), including error model, security semantics, invariants, and state transitions | System Design Authority | Department | 56h | £3,000 |
| Implement information hiding patterns and DTO/view-model layer (remove exposure of persistence entities/internal identifiers/state fields; ensure representation independence) | Software Lead | Department | 48h | — |
| Identify redundant/semantically overloaded interfaces; deprecate with controlled migration plan and enforce strict parameter/schema validation | Software Lead | Department | 32h | — |
| Generate automated contract/conformance tests from contract artefacts (positive/negative/unauthorised access paths; idempotency/retry behaviour checks) and integrate into CI/CD with evidence retention | Quality Assurance Lead | Team | 72h | £6,000 |
| Produce audit-ready evidence pack (design/security records linking contracts to implemented behaviour; retain contract test results and change history) | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 20h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 268h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £9,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a catalogue of version-controlled contracts for all component and service interfaces, and assign an owner for each one.
- [ ] Define clear interface agreements using standard formats such as OpenAPI/AsyncAPI, Protocol Buffers with gRPC, GraphQL schema, or internal interface definitions—making sure they include the expected error behaviour and security requirements.
- [ ] Refactor interface implementations so they only expose the standard data transfer objects (DTOs) and view models, and so no internal persistence or other internal state details are exposed.
- [ ] Find interface definitions that are duplicated or used for different meanings, and then deprecate or remove them using a controlled migration plan.
- [ ] Set up automated contract and compliance tests based on the system interface descriptions, and include them in the continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) pipeline.
- [ ] Store contract artefacts and contract testing evidence in the system design and security documentation set so they can be traced during audits.


---

#### SA-8.2 — Least Common Mechanism (Enhancement)

This expectation is about reducing how much different applications and services depend on the same underlying workings, so they do not accidentally interfere with each other. If multiple parts of a system use the same access method or rely on the same shared information status, a mistake or malicious change in one part can spread and harm other parts that depend on that shared information. This can lead to wrong decisions, disrupted services, or the exposure of sensitive information.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must first identify which components share information across the mission applications and services. It must then design and build them so that the amount of shared mechanisms is kept to the minimum necessary—avoiding shared access routes and shared data wherever possible. Any sharing that remains must be carefully controlled so one application cannot unintentionally affect or compromise another.

The organisation must document the procedures used during planning, design, development, implementation, and changes; keep evidence in design and architecture records; define security and privacy requirements; assign named responsibilities; and keep the supporting documents up to date throughout the system’s lifecycle.

##### Minimise shared state across mission services via bounded contexts

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Conduct shared-state discovery and produce the traceable shared-state map (read/write relationships, data/status domains, producers/consumers, current coupling points) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Define bounded-context/state ownership model and system-of-record boundaries; produce design decisions and technical prohibitions on unauthorised writes (including refactoring plan per domain) | Architect | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Implement bounded-context integration patterns: refactor consumers to maintain local state (read models/projections) and replace direct shared-variable/data writes with event-driven updates | Software Lead | Enterprise | 120h | — |
| Establish narrowly scoped, versioned contracts for shared integrations (schemas, event/API contracts, compatibility rules) and implement contract validation (schema/version enforcement, reject unknown/invalid messages) | Platform Engineer | Enterprise | 56h | — |
| Implement boundary security controls for ingestion: service identity (mTLS/strong auth), authorisation checks, integrity protections, idempotency/replay protection, and strict error handling | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 64h | — |
| Add blast-radius protections for shared integrations: validation hardening, rate limiting, circuit breakers, backpressure/retry strategy, and safe failure modes to prevent cascading corruption | DevOps Lead | Enterprise | 48h | — |
| Produce audit-ready evidence pack: architecture/security design records, shared-state map, contract specs, change records, and least-common-mechanism rationale with review sign-off | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Total Estimated Effort |  |  | **384h** |  |
| Total Estimated Cost |  |  |  | **£0** |

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a shared-data inventory map showing which sa-08.02_odp components read and write the same data or status, and through which methods this happens.
- [ ] Assign one “system of record” (the organisation responsible for the current state) for each shared-state area, and document which parties are not allowed to make changes (write operations) unless they are the owner.
- [ ] Refactor integrations to stop writing directly to shared variables or data. Do this by using separate bounded contexts and updating information through event-driven messages, with each consumer keeping its own state.
- [ ] Use versioned, schema-checked event and application programming interface (API) agreements, and reject any invalid or unknown data at the point where information first enters the system.
- [ ] Require that services authorise each other (workload identity) and that shared integrations protect data integrity (mutual Transport Layer Security (mTLS), replay protection, and the ability to safely repeat requests).
- [ ] Add blast-radius controls (rate limiting, circuit breakers, and input validation) to stop one component’s failure from causing widespread, cascading corruption.
- [ ] Record evidence of the architecture and security design (including the shared-state map, who owns what, contract specifications, and the reasons behind the design) and make sure the change-control process protects the design when changes are made.


---

#### SA-8.3 — Modularity and Layering (Enhancement)

This expectation is about building security as a well-organised set of building blocks, then arranging those blocks into clear layers. This makes it easier to see what depends on what. Without a modular, layered design, weaknesses can spread, updates can accidentally remove or weaken protections, and it becomes harder to ensure only the right people can access Confidential or Secret information. It also becomes harder to make sure the system reliably records what happened and keeps data protected.

The organisation must design and implement all on-premises information technology (IT) and operational technology (OT) systems that handle Confidential and Secret data. It must use separate, clearly defined security components for: proving identity, deciding permissions, recording security-relevant events, and protecting data. These components must be placed into a layered structure with clear boundaries, such as network zones, application tiers, and host layers.

The organisation must set out these principles in its procurement (acquisition) and change procedures. It must assign named responsibilities to the right people, ensure developers and information security staff apply the approach throughout the system’s life, and keep design documents and plans showing the modules, layer responsibilities, dependencies, and isolation boundaries. These records must also include evidence that changes update the relevant design documentation.

##### Modular layered security design for IT/OT Confidential/Secret systems

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define modular security component contracts (authentication, authorisation, logging, data protection) including purpose, inputs/outputs, required privileges, explicit dependencies, and “allowed-to-call” interfaces | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Define layered security architecture model (network zones, application tiers, host layers) and map each modular component to one or more layers with clear boundaries and dependency rules | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Produce per-system modular & layered security design records (audit-ready) including diagrams, module-to-layer mapping, isolation mechanisms, and enforcement-point descriptions | Compliance Manager | Business Unit | 48h | — |
| Implement/verify technical enforcement alignment to the design (segmentation rules, privilege separation where feasible, centralised enforcement points, and isolated logging/audit pipeline) for in-scope environments | Infrastructure Lead | Department | 64h | £25,000 |
| Establish lifecycle governance for design updates (change templates, design record update requirements, evidence retention rules, and release checklist integration) | Project Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Create audit evidence pack and traceability (design record versions, change-linked evidence, module interface/dependency updates, and enforcement verification outputs) for Confidential/Secret systems | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Run design review workshops and sign-off (security architect + platform + system owners) to confirm modularity/layering compliance and resolve dependency/complexity risks | Cyber Security Lead | Enterprise | 24h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 256h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £25,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create separate, reusable security component definitions for each system covering authentication, authorisation, logging, and data protection. For each component, document its interfaces, inputs and outputs, responsible owner, required privileges, and dependencies.
- [ ] Define the layered security approach for each in-scope system (network zones, application layers, and host layers), and assign each security module to the specific responsibilities of the layer it supports.
- [ ] Implement and document technical separation controls, including network segmentation, separating privileges and (where feasible) separating application memory spaces, and using a dedicated, isolated audit and logging system, in line with the design records.
- [ ] Update the acquisition, design, and change procedures so they require modular, layered design references. Also ensure that when changes are made, they update the module interfaces, the responsibilities of each layer, the dependencies between layers, and the boundaries that keep layers isolated from each other.
- [ ] Keep audit-ready records that show the current mapping between each software module and its system layer, and document updates that are linked to changes, for all in-scope on-premises Confidential and Secret information technology (IT) and operational technology (OT) systems.


---

#### SA-8.4 — Partially Ordered Dependencies (Enhancement)

This requirement is about designing systems in clear layers, so that each part only depends on the correct lower-level parts. It is meant to avoid tangled “self-feeding” dependency loops. If this is not done, making changes can become risky and unpredictable, faults can spread more easily, and it becomes harder to test, understand, and fix problems—especially when multiple teams or suppliers are involved.

The organisation must design and implement the application services, middleware components, and underlying platform services so that their security-related dependencies always flow in one direction—from higher layers to lower layers. Each layer should be as self-contained as possible. If any circular dependency loops are unavoidable, they must be kept within a single layer and not allowed to cross between layers.

The organisation must document this approach in its design and security architecture, include it in its security and privacy requirements and its security plan, and use it through the full lifecycle (specification, design, development, implementation, and modification). It must assign named responsibilities and be able to demonstrate, using design records and change records, that dependency direction and circularity checks were carried out.

##### Enforce layered dependency direction with CI/CD cycle checks

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define the layered dependency model (application → middleware → platform) and publish the dependency contract (allowed/forbidden inter-layer directions) for in-scope component types | Solution/Enterprise Architect | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Create the interface-level dependency allowlist approach (approved APIs/SDKs, message topics, shared libraries, DB access patterns) and document mapping rules to layers | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Implement dependency discovery in CI (build-time graph generation for target tech stacks; normalise into a common dependency model) | DevOps Lead | Enterprise | 32h | £6,000 |
| Implement CI/CD gates to fail on reverse-direction dependencies and to detect cross-layer cycles (with clear failure messages and artefact outputs) | Platform Engineer | Enterprise | 28h | £4,000 |
| Optional release-time verification: integrate runtime call graph/telemetry checks where available and correlate to build-time graphs | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 16h | £8,000 |
| Establish waiver/exception process and tooling artefacts (waiver template, approval workflow, expiry, compensating controls, linkage to change records) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 18h | — |
| Evidence and reporting: generate auditable dependency reports (directionality + cycle confinement) and store CI gate outputs/waivers for audit | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 14h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 142h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £18,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the service layers for application services, middleware components, and platform services, and publish a dependency agreement.
- [ ] Create a list of allowed links between layers (inter-layer dependency edges), and explicitly block any links that go in the reverse direction.
- [ ] Set up automated generation of dependency maps in the build process for each component or layer.
- [ ] Add continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) gates to stop cross-layer reverse dependencies and to detect and report cross-layer cycles.
- [ ] Set up a waiver process that requires approval from the architecture and security teams, includes a risk assessment and compensating controls, has an expiry date, and is linked to the change record.
- [ ] Update the security architecture and security plan to reference the dependency contract, and to store continuous integration (CI) gate results and waiver evidence so they are available for audit.


---

#### SA-8.5 — Efficiently Mediated Access (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure access decisions are checked in the right place, using the simplest effective approach, so only the right people and systems can reach the resources they are authorised to use. Without this, organisations can end up with repeated or overly complicated checks that slow systems down, or with gaps where access is not properly controlled—raising the risk of unauthorised access to data, services, devices, or infrastructure.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must identify all on-premises access decision points, including host or virtualisation policy controls, network firewalls, application programming interface (API) gateways, and identity and access services. It must also define how “efficiently mediated access” will be applied: use the least complex enforcement method that still meets stakeholder needs and any stated constraints, and design it so it does not create avoidable performance bottlenecks.

The organisation must build this into system security requirements, architecture, and plans; ensure it is followed during specification, design, development, implementation, and changes; keep clear records of the reasons for the approach and the performance considerations; and require named responsible people to oversee it during procurement and delivery.

##### Policy-as-code enforcement point selection with performance budgets

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Produce enforcement point catalogue for on-prem in-scope components (IAM/IAM-integrated services, API gateways, network firewalls, host/hypervisor policy engines) including resource types and mediation locations | Security Architect | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Define decision matrix mapping access decision types to least-complex enforcement point(s), including stakeholder requirements/constraints and explicit rationale | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Implement policy-as-code model for enforcement point selection and rationale binding (including performance budget fields and required traversal constraints) | Software Lead | Enterprise | 56h | — |
| Build CI/CD enforcement gates to block redundant/overly complex mediation and to require performance budget documentation/validation (including exception workflow hooks) | DevOps Lead | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Implement runtime telemetry instrumentation and detection-as-code checks for bypass patterns (latency/error measurement, policy decision outcomes, expected enforcement point traversal) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 48h | £8,000 |
| Create audit-ready evidence packaging for each system change (catalogue references, decision matrix rationale, policy-as-code outputs, CI/CD gate results, runtime validation outputs) | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Pilot rollout on representative on-prem system(s) and tune performance budgets/thresholds based on measured p95 latency and CPU headroom impact; produce go-live readiness report | Operations Lead | Business Unit | 32h | £3,000 |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 264h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £11,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an enforcement point catalogue for on-premises components (hypervisor/host policy engines, network firewalls, application programming interface (API) gateways, and identity and access management (IAM) services). Then match each type of resource to where it should be mediated.
- [ ] Define and get approval for a decision matrix that, for each type of access decision, chooses the simplest enforcement method that meets stakeholder requirements. Include the constraints and the reasons for each choice.
- [ ] Set up “policy as code” so that every access decision is linked to the chosen enforcement point(s), and so that mediation performance is tracked against a defined budget.
- [ ] Add continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) policy checks to prevent unnecessary repeat mediation and to require evidence that each release meets the agreed performance budget.
- [ ] Set up enforcement points with monitoring data (telemetry) to measure how long mediation takes and how often errors occur, and to confirm that runtime policy checks are being followed correctly.
- [ ] Create audit-ready evidence packs for each change. Each pack must include: the reason for the enforcement decision, any limitations or constraints, proof that performance was validated, and the results from the continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) approval checks.


---

#### SA-8.6 — Minimized Sharing (Enhancement)

This requirement is about keeping different parts of a system from using the same underlying information or working space unless it is genuinely necessary. If this is not done, one user, service, or function could accidentally or deliberately view, change, or misuse another part’s information, or take advantage of hidden effects that reveal what should be kept separate. This increases the risk of unauthorised access, disclosure, misuse, or tampering.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must design, build, operate, and change each information system so that user-related resources are not shared by default between different system components. Any sharing must only be allowed when there is a clear, documented need, with an explicit request and formal authorisation.

The organisation must avoid unnecessary shared “common” mechanisms, such as widely used global data. Where sharing is required, it must be carefully engineered to preserve separation and prevent hidden leaks.

The organisation must document these decisions and apply the principle throughout the work, including during specification and changes. It must include it in security requirements, design records, architecture, and plans, assign named responsibilities, define the supporting processes and mechanisms, and keep evidence in the relevant records.

##### Isolation-by-default with explicit, authorised sharing contracts

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define “user-domain resources” boundaries (data objects, session/in-memory state, workflow state, service capabilities) and document separation rules (no cross-subject/process/function access by default) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Produce isolation-by-default architecture design: isolated execution contexts and isolated data planes (namespaces/accounts/tenants, strict RBAC mapping, removal of cross-component shared storage paths) | Architect | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Identify and eliminate implicit/common sharing paths (global data, shared temp directories, broadly accessible caches, shared DB schemas) and specify required remediations with implementation-ready acceptance criteria | Security Engineer | Department | 40h | — |
| Implement explicit sharing contracts: define narrowly scoped interfaces (APIs/queues/events) with strict schemas, boundary authorisation checks, stateless/context-bound handling, and traceable contract identifiers | Software Lead | Department | 56h | — |
| Implement reentrancy/virtualisation patterns for shared mechanisms (per-request/per-tenant context objects, correlation IDs bound to caller identity, removal of mutable global state) and add defensive checks to prevent context mixing/covert leakage | Security Engineer | Department | 40h | — |
| Build policy-as-code guardrails for “no sharing by default” (CI/CD and deployment checks that fail builds when broad cross-component access or unapproved sharing contracts are configured) | DevOps Lead | Enterprise | 48h | £8,000 |
| Develop and run automated separation tests (negative cross-component access denial + side-effect absence checks for high-risk flows) and integrate into regression pipeline | Quality Assurance Lead | Team | 40h | — |
| Create audit-ready evidence pack per sharing contract (approval/authorisation record, design rationale, request/response logs, and test evidence demonstrating separation) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 304h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £8,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and document the system-specific set of user-related resources, and the component boundaries that must not share those resources.
- [ ] Refactor the system’s architecture and configuration to ensure isolation by default. This means running components in separate execution environments and using separate data pathways, and removing any shared storage locations that components currently use across one another.
- [ ] Identify and remove any built-in or commonly used ways that data can be shared automatically (for example, shared global data or shared temporary/cache folders), or restrict them by using strict separation controls.
- [ ] Create clear sharing agreements for interfaces (application programming interfaces (APIs), message queues, and events), with permission checks at the boundary, and use tightly limited data formats (schemas).
- [ ] Implement re-entrancy and virtualisation patterns for any shared components that are required, to prevent information from being mixed between users or sessions and to stop hidden data leakage.
- [ ] Add policy-as-code controls that block deployments that would introduce unauthorised sharing between components.
- [ ] Develop and run automated separation tests to confirm that one component cannot access another, and that there are no noticeable or observable side effects.


---

#### SA-8.7 — Reduced Complexity (Enhancement)

This requirement is about keeping the security parts of your on-site systems as simple and as small as possible, so they are easier to understand and harder to get wrong. If security designs become too complicated, people are more likely to make mistakes, important security features can be missed, and weaknesses can be introduced during building, deploying, or changing the system—especially in mixed environments where different types of equipment and software must work together.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must apply the reduced-complexity approach to all on-premises mixed information technology (IT) and operational technology (OT) systems and components that carry out security functions. It must also create clear written procedures describing how this simplicity is considered during planning, design, development, implementation, and any later changes.

The organisation must assign named responsibilities, use practical checks such as review steps and approval gates to confirm simplicity before changes go live, and keep design and planning records showing how the approach was applied and carried into the deployed configuration. This includes any time-limited periods where complexity may temporarily increase during transitions.

##### Reduced-complexity security design gates for IT/OT security functions

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define SA-8(7) scope inventory for on-prem mixed IT/OT security-function components (including ownership, system/component boundaries, and evidence location) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Translate “reduced complexity” into a small set of measurable, reviewable design constraints (with acceptance thresholds and examples for common IT/OT security patterns) | Security Architect | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Update lifecycle templates (security architecture review pack and change-pack/CAB workflow) to include mandatory **Complexity Delta** and **Transition Exception** sections with required fields and sign-off prompts | Project Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Create and publish the “Reduced Complexity” gated design review checklist (mapping each constraint to review questions, required artefacts, and approval outcomes) | Security Architect | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Integrate the gate into engineering design review / CAB process (workflow steps, mandatory checklist completion, and escalation path when exceptions are requested) | Service Delivery Manager | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Establish evidence capture and retention approach (naming conventions, storage locations, traceability to deployed configuration baseline, and audit-ready packaging of diagrams/deltas/validation outputs) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Pilot the gate on a representative set of security-function changes across IT/OT (including exception handling), then refine constraints/checklist based on findings | Security Engineer | Business Unit | 40h | — |
| Define named responsibilities and operating model (RACI, approval authority, training/briefings for architects, CAB chairs, and security approvers; go-live readiness) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 220h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep an up-to-date list of on-premises mixed information technology (IT) and operational technology (OT) systems and components that carry out security functions (within the SA-8(7) scope).
- [ ] Publish simpler engineering limits and clear, measurable rules for security design—for example, reduce the number of security components, reduce the number of trust boundaries, and avoid duplicating policy logic.
- [ ] Update the security architecture and change-request templates so they require a “Complexity Delta” explanation and, where applicable, a time-limited “Transition Exception” section.
- [ ] For any change to a security function, make sure the engineering design review and change advisory board (CAB) process includes mandatory, simplified checklist items.
- [ ] Set up specific named roles for completing “Complexity Delta”, carrying out the review, and approving any exceptions, and record each approval in the change management system.
- [ ] Set up evidence retention that links the approved, simpler supporting materials (such as diagrams, written explanations, and policy or configuration summaries) to the configuration baseline that has been deployed.


---

#### SA-8.8 — Secure Evolvability (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure a system’s security protections keep working—and can be improved safely—whenever the system changes, such as during upgrades, maintenance, reconfiguration, or when new capabilities are added. Without this, a change made for business reasons could unintentionally weaken protections, disrupt how security rules are applied, or create new weaknesses by changing how parts of the system connect. This could leave the organisation exposed in ways that were never tested or planned for.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must create and follow clear, written procedures for planning and carrying out security-safe changes across the entire system life cycle—from requirements and design, through development, implementation, and later updates or modifications. It must document how security will remain easy to maintain and update, set out security and privacy requirements that explain how changes will be handled, and keep a security plan and system design that show how security protections will be preserved.

Named individuals must be responsible for setting the requirements, applying the approach during build and change activities, and supporting checks and verification. Planning must be based on likely future mission direction, expected changes in threats, and maintenance needs—not on ad hoc decisions.

##### Secure evolvability change gates for mission systems and policy enforcement

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Establish secure evolvability change-gate framework (Gate A–D) including entry/exit criteria, roles/approvals, evidence requirements, and audit retention rules | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Define and publish secure evolvability procedures for the full lifecycle (specify/design, develop, implement, modify) including security impact analysis templates and security design record update requirements | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Implement gated CI/CD workflow: enforce gate checks, approval routing, evidence capture, and release blocking for in-scope changes (mission systems, interfaces, security policy enforcement components) | DevOps Lead | Enterprise | 56h | £18,000 |
| Implement versioned, testable security policy enforcement configuration (policy-as-code / infrastructure-as-code modules) with traceability to requirements and change records | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 48h | £6,000 |
| Build security regression test suite and interface contract tests for each change type (authn/authz, encryption in transit, logging/telemetry, least-privilege boundaries, schema/API compatibility) | Software Lead | Enterprise | 72h | £10,000 |
| Create rollback/recovery expectations and validate rollback evidence (known-good security configuration, rollback runbooks, and rollback testing criteria integrated into Gate C evidence) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Pilot the full gated release workflow on 1–2 representative mission system releases; remediate gaps; then roll out standard operating procedure for all in-scope systems | Project Manager | Enterprise | 40h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 286h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £34,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish secure “evolvability” procedures for mission systems, including how to specify and design, develop, implement, and update: (1) the systems themselves, (2) their interfaces, and (3) the components that enforce security policy.
- [ ] Set up a controlled (gated) continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) release process that requires a security impact assessment, a plan to update security policies and configuration, and named approvals before any deployment.
- [ ] Implement policy and configuration changes as version-controlled, testable configuration packages (for example, policy-as-code and infrastructure-as-code modules), with clear traceability back to the original requirements.
- [ ] Add automated tests for interface contracts and security regressions to confirm—after every change—that authentication and authorisation work correctly, data is protected while travelling (encryption in transit), logging and telemetry are recorded, and least-privilege access boundaries are maintained.
- [ ] Update the system security plan and the security architecture/design records as part of the release evidence pack. If this is deferred, record the deferral with an expiry date and a named accountable owner.
- [ ] For security changes that could affect protection, define and test a rollback and recovery route back to a previously confirmed “known-good” security setup, and keep records showing the rollback was carried out.


---

#### SA-8.9 — Trusted Components (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that every part used in your security systems is genuinely reliable for the job it does, and that this reliability is not weakened when parts are combined. If it is not, a weak or insufficiently checked component could undermine key protections such as verifying who someone is, protecting data, recording events, applying updates, or controlling access to networks—leaving the organisation exposed even if everything else is well designed.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- identify all systems and components that provide security functions or depend on them;
- decide how reliable each one must be based on how important it is;
- use a consistent method to measure both the required reliability and the component’s actual reliability.

It must also:
- document how reliability is transferred between components;
- treat combined systems conservatively, assuming the overall reliability is no higher than the least reliable part unless there is clear, evidence-based justification;
- make these checks part of procurement, design, development, implementation, and any changes.

Decisions and the evidence supporting them must be recorded in the relevant project and planning documents.

##### Trusted component assurance levels with dependency-aware composition

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Establish trusted component assurance scope and component inventory approach (including environments: prod/test/dev/DR) and define what qualifies as “security function provider/dependency” | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Create organisation-approved trustworthiness scale (e.g., AL1–AL4) with explicit, evidenceable criteria and mapping to security function types (identity, crypto, logging/audit, patching/updates, network security, etc.) | Security Engineering Lead | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Define required trust levels per security dependency role and produce the trust mapping matrix (component type → required trust level based on dependency chain) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Model trust dependencies and document trust relationships for compound components (architecture/design artefacts + component manifests), including default “minimum subcomponent trustworthiness” rule | Architect | Department | 48h | — |
| Implement evidence collection requirements and templates (SBOM/signed artefacts/attestations/assessment reports/vulnerability responsiveness/secure update mechanism) and define acceptable evidence sources | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Create exception workflow for conservative-by-default overrides (security engineering rationale, credible evidence requirements, approval routing, audit trail) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Integrate trust checks into change/release gates (CAB/release process updates, required fields in change records, verification steps, and remediation triggers when evidence/levels do not meet requirements) | Project Manager | Business Unit | 56h | — |

Total Estimated Effort: **256h**  
Total Estimated Cost: **£0**

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep an up-to-date list of every system and component that either provides security functions for the application or relies on security functions, across all environments.
- [ ] Define an organisation-approved trustworthiness scale with clear, verifiable criteria, and share it with the engineering and procurement teams.
- [ ] Create a mapping from each security function to the level of trust it requires, then apply that mapping to every component based on how that component depends on security.
- [ ] Document the “trust dependency chains” (including combined or nested components) in architecture and design documents, and in component inventory files (manifests).
- [ ] Use a “trust conservatively by default” approach for combined components: the overall trustworthiness is determined by the least trustworthy part.
- [ ] Set up an exception process that requires a security engineering justification and reliable supporting evidence when proposing that something be treated as more trustworthy than its least trustworthy part.
- [ ] Add trust-level and evidence checks to the application’s change and release approval process, so component upgrades cannot be approved unless the required trust levels are met.


---

#### SA-8.10 — Hierarchical Trust (Enhancement)

This requirement is about setting up trust in your system in a clear, one-way chain. The most basic parts must be trusted first, and no part should end up depending on another part in a circular way. Without this, a weak or compromised component could be used to “back up” the safety of stronger parts, making it harder to be confident that the whole system is secure as it is put together from different elements.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must:
- design, build, and maintain a documented hierarchy of the system’s software components and the underlying hardware or firmware trust anchors, with no circular trust dependencies; and
- be able to explain the direction of reliance, starting from the lowest layer and moving upwards.

You must apply this approach throughout your acquisition and change processes, including during specification, design, development, implementation, and modification. You must also keep evidence in your records for design, security and privacy requirements, architecture, and security plans.

If a more trusted component is intentionally used in a lower layer even though it relies on a less trusted one, you must document and obtain approval for the justification, so the overall trust position is not weakened.

##### Hierarchical trust dependency matrix with circular-trust checks

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Confirm SA-8(10) scope boundaries (sa-08.10_odp) and define the trust scope boundaries across hardware/firmware, boot chain, OS/runtime, middleware, and security-critical application components | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 8h | — |
| Produce component inventory and trust-relevant artefact list (e.g., secure boot/TPM attestation verification points, signed artefact verification rules, bootloader/firmware update verification, OS/runtime trust decisions) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Create the hierarchical trust dependency matrix (component → specific lower-layer trust mechanism relied upon; include explicit direction from trust anchors upwards) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Define the one-way trust ordering rule and derive the dependency graph from the matrix (including direct and indirect dependencies) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Perform circular-trust loop check on the derived dependency graph; document findings, identify any cycles, and prepare either remediation actions or exception justifications | Cyber Security Lead | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| If cycles exist, prepare exception packs for CCB approval (justification, impact assessment on trustworthiness reasoning, compensating controls, and acceptance criteria) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Publish assurance evidence: integrate matrix + loop-check outputs into system security architecture and system security plan records; update configuration baseline references and create an auditable change-control checklist gate | Project Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |

| Total Estimated Effort | 164h | Total Estimated Cost | £0 |

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the levels of trust for the information system, software components, and the hardware and firmware trust anchors.
- [ ] Create an inventory of the boot process components, trust anchors, operating system and runtime components, middleware, and security-critical application components.
- [ ] Create a trust dependency matrix that shows, for each component, the specific lower-layer trust mechanism it depends on.
- [ ] Run and record a circular-trust loop check on the derived trust dependency map.
- [ ] Document and get approval for any intentional exception where a lower-level, highly trusted component relies on a higher-level, less trusted component.
- [ ] Publish the hierarchy evidence (matrix, diagram, and loop-check evidence) in the system security architecture records and the system security plan records.
- [ ] Add a change-control step that requires updating the trust matrix and re-checking the trust verification loop for any change that affects trust anchors or trust verification.


---

#### SA-8.11 — Inverse Modification Threshold (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the most trusted parts of your security setup are also the hardest to tamper with. If a highly trusted component can be changed without strong safeguards, attackers could quietly alter how keys are handled or how access is granted. This could lead to unauthorised access, fraud, or the ability to bypass protections.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set out how it will apply the “more trust means stronger protection” approach to cryptographic modules, key management services, and identity and access management components. It must ensure that as the level of trust increases, so does the strength of protection against unauthorised changes.

This should be done by using the component’s built-in safeguards where appropriate, and by adding further protections from other parts of the security design and from the environment where the component runs.

The organisation must document this across the full lifecycle in procurement and change procedures, record the design decisions in system documents, assign named responsibilities, and be able to demonstrate that higher-trust components receive stronger safeguards and stricter change approvals, with evidence that these protections are actually in place and enabled.

##### Trust-tiered tamper resistance for crypto, KMS and IAM components

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define trust-tier model for in-scope component types (cryptographic modules, KMS, IAM), including tier criteria (self-protection/innate trustworthiness, key sensitivity, blast radius, role in authz/authn) and tier assignment method | Security Architect | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Define tier-to-modification-resistance mapping (inverse modification threshold) with enforceable requirements per tier (e.g., signed artefact enforcement, integrity attestation, restricted admin interfaces, change approval strictness, separation of duties) | Security Architect | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Produce control implementation standards and evidence requirements (design artefacts, attestation outputs, signed verification results, immutable/append-only audit logging evidence, approval/workflow logs) and update lifecycle templates (design, change, acquisition) to reference the mapping | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Implement workflow enforcement in delivery/operations (policy-as-code checks in CI/CD, configuration management guardrails, deployment gating, and approval workflow rules that prevent higher-trust components bypassing tier requirements) | DevOps Lead | Enterprise | 32h | £6,000 |
| Implement self-protection controls for each component type where available (signed updates/configuration, integrity checks, hardware-backed key storage/attestation hooks, locked-down admin surfaces) and document per-tier implementation evidence | Security Engineer | Department | 28h | £12,000 |
| Implement architectural/environmental protections that scale by trust tier (hardened hosting profiles, restricted network paths to admin endpoints, separation of approvers vs deployers, immutable audit logging, controlled CI/CD pathways) | Infrastructure Lead | Enterprise | 24h | £10,000 |
| Validate end-to-end effectiveness via tabletop and audit-ready walkthroughs (map evidence to tiers, test deployment gating, verify attestation/signature checks, confirm audit log immutability and retention) and remediate gaps | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 16h | — |

Total Estimated Effort: **160 Hours**  
Total Estimated Cost: **£28,000**

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define a trust-level model for cryptographic modules, key management systems (KMS), and identity and access management (IAM) components, including clear trust requirements and definitions for each trust level.
- [ ] Assign each trust level to specific, verifiable requirements for making changes harder to tamper with, with the requirements becoming stronger at higher trust levels.
- [ ] Put in place self-protection for the higher-trust levels, for example: only allow signed software updates and configuration changes, perform integrity checks and verification (attestation), and restrict administrative features to a locked-down set.
- [ ] Put in place security measures in the system design and hosting environment that increase as the trust level increases—for example, limit which network routes administrators can use, use more secure hosting, separate responsibilities between different roles, and keep audit records that cannot be changed once written.
- [ ] Apply the tier mapping during continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) and configuration management by using policy checks and requiring approval before changes can be released.
- [ ] Update the system security architecture and design documents, and the acquisition and change procedures, to record the tier mapping and the required evidence outputs.


---

#### SA-8.12 — Hierarchical Protection (Enhancement)

This requirement is about strengthening the parts of your system that you trust most, so they provide the main protection—rather than relying on every other part to protect itself.

In practice, if the most trusted components (such as the operating system kernel and other top-level system parts) do not clearly enforce what each application is allowed to do, a harmful or badly behaved application could:
- access things it should not,
- change important settings, or
- weaken the whole environment—especially if users are not fully trustworthy.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must:
- clearly identify which components are the most trusted,
- document who is protected by whom, and what protections are expected, and
- reflect this in your system design, security plans, and architecture records.

You must also ensure your purchasing and change processes require this approach at every stage: specification, design, build, deployment, and updates. Named people must be responsible for setting the requirements and providing oversight.

Finally, you must keep evidence that changes were tested and verified, so less-trusted applications remain blocked by the trusted components, and so applications are not expected to provide the same level of protection as the kernel.

##### Kernel/most-trusted components enforce boundaries for less-trusted apps

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define and publish the trust hierarchy in the system security architecture (kernel/most-trusted vs less-trusted application components) and map each boundary to the enforcing kernel/OS subsystem | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Produce boundary responsibility statements for: process privilege, filesystem access, device access, IPC mediation, network ingress/egress mediation, and credential handling (explicitly stating enforcement is not delegated to application logic) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 12h | — |
| Select and standardise OS-level enforcement mechanisms per platform (e.g., mandatory access control, least-privilege execution, syscall/device mediation, OS sandboxing/containment) and define configuration baselines | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Implement kernel/OS enforcement controls and integrate with deployment templates (least-privilege service accounts, restricted filesystem/secret access, device/interface restrictions, network mediation policies) | Platform Engineer | Business Unit | 40h | £0 |
| Develop “kernel denies” acceptance criteria and automated boundary test cases (normal-user execution contexts; prohibited actions: secret read, protected path write, restricted device access, privilege escalation attempts) | Quality Assurance Lead | Department | 24h | — |
| Execute boundary verification tests in release validation and collect audit-ready evidence (test logs, OS control decision outputs, denial confirmations, reproducible test steps) | Operations Lead | Department | 16h | — |
| Establish change-control re-verification process for OS/host security component and deployment configuration changes (triggered re-runs, documentation updates, evidence retention) | Project Manager | Enterprise | 12h | — |

Total Estimated Effort: **144h**  
Total Estimated Cost: **£0**

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and get approval for a “trust hierarchy” section in the system security design. In this section, identify the operating system kernel and other key, highly trusted components as the most trusted, and classify the application components as less trusted.
- [ ] Define clear responsibility statements for each protection boundary, including who is responsible for enforcing it, what is blocked, and what evidence will be collected.
- [ ] Set up operating system (OS) controls to enforce least-privilege execution and use mandatory access controls and sandboxing, so the operating system mediates and enforces the security boundaries through its built-in security features.
- [ ] Write release validation test cases that try prohibited actions from a less-trusted application context and confirm that the attempt is blocked at the operating system (OS) level.
- [ ] Add boundary verification checks to the software release process, and keep the resulting logs and files as evidence for audits.
- [ ] Re-run boundary checks and update the architecture and security documentation whenever the operating system kernel or host security components change, or when the deployment configuration changes.


---

#### SA-8.13 — Minimized Security Elements (Enhancement)

This requirement is about keeping the number of “highly trusted” parts of your system as small as possible, so fewer components need to be relied on to keep things secure. If an organisation adds unnecessary trusted services, it becomes harder to show they are safe, easier for mistakes to slip in, and easier for attackers to take advantage of weaknesses through complex links between those trusted parts.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must design, build, run, and update the information system and its trusted components (such as identity services, key handling, and access control services) using the principle of minimising trusted components. It must not add extra trusted components unless there is a clear need.

The organisation must also write and follow procedures that apply this approach during planning, design, development, deployment, and any changes. It must assign named people responsibility for these decisions, and keep current design and security planning documents that show which trusted components are used, why they are necessary, and that their interactions are kept simple.

##### Trusted component inventory with approval gate and interaction mapping

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define “trusted component” taxonomy and scope for SA-8(13) (including boundaries, examples, and inclusion/exclusion rules) and publish as an architecture standard addendum | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Design the trusted component inventory data model (fields, versioning, evidence links, approval status, and interaction mapping structure) and create the inventory template | Architect | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Create and populate the initial trusted component inventory for the application scope (identify components, owners, trust boundaries, security functions, assurance/verification approach, and interaction links) | Cyber Security Lead | Department | 40h | — |
| Implement the justification gate workflow (intake form, required rationale prompts, approval criteria, and audit trail requirements) for each trusted component | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Implement delivery pipeline gate (policy-as-code/workflow checks that block merges/releases introducing or expanding trusted components unless inventory + justification are updated and approved) | DevOps Lead | Department | 56h | £6,000 |
| Define and standardise “interaction simplicity evidence” (interaction map format, verification note template, and review checklist) and run review for current inventory items | Security Engineer | Department | 24h | — |
| Establish change-through-maintenance process (trigger conditions, periodic review cadence, evidence capture for representative recent change, and operational ownership) | Project Manager | Business Unit | 24h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 208h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £6,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish the system-specific definition of a “trusted component” in line with sa-08.13_odp.
- [ ] Create and fill in a trusted component inventory for the information system, including the responsible owners and the defined trust boundaries.
- [ ] Create a justification record for each trusted component. In the record, explain why the component is necessary and why no alternative options are being used.
- [ ] Document the trust links for each trusted component, showing who relies on whom and what is checked or verified.
- [ ] Set up a release and design approval checkpoint that prevents any new or expanded “trusted” components from being added unless the component list (inventory) and the reasons for using them are updated and approved.
- [ ] Update the inventory and re-approve the justifications for any change that affects identity, key management, authorisation or policy enforcement, or the system’s trust boundaries.


---

#### SA-8.14 — Least Privilege (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring every part of your systems only has the permissions it genuinely needs to do its job—and no more. Otherwise, a mistake, misuse, or compromise in one component could spread further than necessary, allowing unauthorised people to change settings, view sensitive records, or disrupt critical functions such as logging and handling encryption keys.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must design, build, operate, and update all information systems and the parts that affect security. This includes identity and access controls, audit and logging, encryption key management, and administrative interfaces. Permissions must be kept to the minimum needed.

This approach must be built into the organisation’s way of working throughout the entire lifecycle—from setting requirements and designing the system, through development and implementation, and then through later changes. It must also be clearly documented in the system’s security and privacy requirements, architecture, security plan, and related procurement and design records.

##### Least-privilege role engineering with interface-scoped authorisation

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Produce interface-to-action authorisation contract for all security-relevant interfaces (admin consoles, audit config endpoints, audit data access/view endpoints, key/certificate management APIs, privileged operational endpoints), including per-role allowed actions and deny-by-default rules | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Engineer RBAC/ABAC entitlements and role mappings in the enterprise identity provider (including least-privilege role sets for audit reviewer (read-only), audit operator, key-management roles, and exclusion of unrelated admin/key capabilities) | IAM Lead | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Implement server-side authorisation enforcement at the application boundary for every security-relevant operation (controller/service/method mapping to the contract; deny-by-default; remove reliance on UI/client-side controls) | Software Lead | Department | 56h | — |
| Refactor internal module boundaries for least privilege (encapsulation of audit ingestion/storage, read-only audit viewing path, and key material access confined to key-management module; implement minimal access modes and internal permission checks) | Architect | Department | 40h | — |
| Build automated permission regression tests (role/interface/action matrix tests; negative tests for prohibited actions; CI integration; test evidence suitable for audit) | Quality Assurance Lead | Team | 48h | — |
| Implement periodic permission drift detection and reporting (scheduled checks comparing effective IdP roles/entitlements and application authorisation rules against the documented minimums; generate drift reports and remediation tickets) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | £6,000 |
| Document the least-privilege design and evidence pack (system security architecture/design records, system security plan updates, contract + role mapping + enforcement approach, and attach test results and drift review records) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 264h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £6,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set up an agreement that links each security-relevant interface to the actions it is allowed to perform, covering: administration, audit configuration, access to audit data, and key and certificate management.
- [ ] Set up narrowly defined user roles and permissions in the enterprise identity provider (IdP), mapped exactly to the contract requirements. This includes read-only audit reviewer access and restricted key-management roles.
- [ ] Put in place server-side access checks that block by default for every security-sensitive action, and link each action to the relevant contract.
- [ ] Restructure the application modules so each module contains only what it needs, and use the minimum level of permissions (read or write) required for its responsibilities.
- [ ] Create and run automated permission checks for each role to confirm that actions you do not allow are blocked and actions you do allow work correctly.
- [ ] Set up regular checks to confirm that users’ actual access permissions match the documented minimum permissions required for their roles.
- [ ] Publish and keep evidence in the system security architecture and design records, and in the system security plan, showing how responsibilities, connections between components, and test results relate to each other.


---

#### SA-8.15 — Predicate Permission (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that the most sensitive access and the most damaging administrative changes cannot be approved or carried out by a single person working alone. It addresses the real-world risk that one mistake, deception, or misuse of authority could allow access to Secret and Classified (Secret) information, or carry out a high-impact change that is difficult to reverse.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must design and run its privileged access request process and approval checks for Secret and Classified (Secret) data access and high-impact administrative actions so that more than one authorised person must agree before anything can proceed. This must happen either at the same time or through a defined step-by-step sequence, where each step depends on the previous one.

The organisation must apply this control throughout the full system lifecycle—from planning and design, through building and deployment, and including later changes. It must also keep clear records and documentation showing how approvals are enforced, including evidence that at least two separate authorised entities approved the same request before access was activated or the action was completed.

##### Enforce 2+ approver predicate permission for Secret access & high-impact admin

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define in-scope predicates and actions for Secret/Classified (Secret) access and high-impact admin (including simultaneous vs sequential multi-approval models and time windows) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Perform workflow impact assessment and design the PAM orchestration gate (state machine, request binding, self-approval prevention, distinct approver enforcement, and predicate-to-resource mapping) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Implement enforcement in PAM workflow (block token/session issuance and block high-impact actions until required approvals are present; bind approvals to request parameters and specific predicate) | Software Lead | Enterprise | 56h | — |
| Implement approval-policy immutability and separation of duties (version-controlled approval logic, change controls, and safeguards to prevent weakening/altering predicate-permission rules) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Implement audit evidence generation and SIEM export (per-request audit records: request ID, predicate/action, target, environment, distinct approver identities, timestamps, execution/denial outcome) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Develop and execute test plan (unit/integration tests, negative tests for bypass attempts, approval replay prevention, environment segregation checks) and produce evidence pack for assessment | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Deployment, rollout, and operational readiness (release to prod-like environments, runbooks, monitoring/alerting for gate failures, and handover to operations) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 16h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 208h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define which access rules apply to in-scope Secret/Classified (Secret) information, and define the rules for high-impact administrative actions, mapped to privileged access management (PAM) request types.
- [ ] Set up privileged access management (PAM) approval rules so that changes require 2 out of 2 (or 2 out of 3) separate authorised approvals, using either a shared time window and/or a required order of steps.
- [ ] Apply the permission check gate so that issuing privileged tokens or sessions and carrying out actions are blocked until the required approvals are in place.
- [ ] Set up identity binding so that a person cannot approve their own request, and so every approval is linked to the exact details of the request—its target, environment, action, and time window.
- [ ] Set up version-control approval policy rules and apply separation of duties so that changes to the approval logic are made by authorised people in different roles.
- [ ] Enable and export audit records showing the request ID, the identities of the approvers, the times approvals were made, and the times the actions were executed or activated. Send these records to security information and event management (SIEM) for evidence.


---

#### SA-8.16 — Self-reliant Trustworthiness (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure the systems that protect your business do not have to constantly “check in” with outside services to remain trustworthy. In practice, if a system’s security relies on a working connection to an external provider, an outage, interference, or tampering could cause the system to stop enforcing protections or to become less secure.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must identify every system part that must be trustworthy for its security functions, and then design and build it so it is secure by default and can keep working safely on its own. External connections should be used only to add extra capability, not to maintain the basic trust needed for security.

The organisation must also define what happens if the system becomes isolated from external services, and ensure it can reconnect and resynchronise safely without weakening security.

Finally, these requirements must be built into how systems are specified, designed, developed, implemented, and changed. There must be clear records showing that the approach was considered and verified.

##### Design offline-capable security functions with safe resynchronisation

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| In-scope inventory of trustworthy security functions and dependency classification (baseline vs supplementary) with documented trust boundaries | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Define local baseline authoritative sources for each security function (e.g., hardware-backed trust anchors, signed policy bundles, integrity-protected cached decisions) and specify isolation-mode behaviour (deny-by-default / restricted last-known-good with integrity/expiry) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 56h | — |
| Design safe resynchronisation protocol (versioning/sequence numbers, replay protection, authenticated update verification, conflict-handling rules that preserve security invariants) | Architect | Enterprise | 48h | — |
| Implement offline-capable enforcement and isolation mode in the application/security functions (including “dependency unavailable” handling and no fail-open safeguards) | Software Lead | Business Unit | 120h | — |
| Implement reconciliation/resynchronisation pipeline (authenticated update ingestion, replay/version checks, atomic apply/rollback, and restricted-mode exit criteria) | DevOps Lead | Business Unit | 96h | — |
| Build and execute outage/resynchronisation test evidence (automated tests + simulated dependency outages; produce audit-ready results and traceability to SA-8(16)) | Quality Assurance Lead | Department | 72h | £8,000 |
| Update architecture/security design documentation and runbook evidence (trust boundaries, isolation behaviour, resynchronisation rules, operational monitoring/alerts for reconciliation state) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 32h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 464h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £8,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an inventory of all in-scope systems and components ({{ insert: param, sa-08.16_odp }}) and link each one to the security functions that depend on it being trustworthy.
- [ ] Define the limits of trusted access for each security function, and specify the local “source of truth” systems used to enforce the baseline security settings.
- [ ] For each external dependency, label it as either **supplementary** or **required as a baseline**. Then, for any dependency that is **required as a baseline**, redesign it so it is **not needed to establish trust**.
- [ ] Set up a clear “isolation mode” for each security function so it cannot accidentally allow access if something goes wrong (for example, block by default, or allow only a previously verified “last known good” result that is checked for integrity and has not expired).
- [ ] Set up authenticated resynchronisation that includes protection against replayed or out-of-date messages, and apply clear conflict-handling rules to ensure security requirements are maintained.
- [ ] Update the architecture and security design documents to record the trust boundaries, how systems are kept separate, and the rules for how they resynchronise after disruption. Then run outage tests to produce evidence suitable for audit.


---

#### SA-8.17 — Secure Distributed Composition (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that when multiple parts of a distributed system are designed to apply the same security rules, the whole system protects as effectively as each part does on its own. If this is not done, the way components work together can unintentionally weaken protection, creating gaps that could allow unauthorised access, incorrect handling of sensitive information, or inconsistent application of security rules.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly identify which distributed components are intended to enforce the same security policy. It must then apply this approach using documented, repeatable procedures across specification, design, development, implementation, and any changes.

The organisation must also produce and keep up to date design and security architecture documents, together with security and privacy requirements and specifications. These documents must show that the overall system has been analysed to confirm consistent, system-wide enforcement. Evidence of this analysis must be recorded in the system security plan and in supporting records.

##### Secure distributed composition via policy contract and composition test gates

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define the “policy contract” component set and security policy scope mapping (including authorisation decision point, encryption-in-transit, audit/logging, input validation, deny-by-default rules) | Security Engineering Lead | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Produce version-controlled policy-as-code artefacts for the shared policy (templates, schema, versioning, change control hooks, and enforcement configuration guidance) | Security Engineering Lead | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Implement/verify consistent enforcement across inter-service calls (e.g., central authorisation checks, service-to-service identity propagation, deny-by-default, protocol downgrade protections) | Platform Engineer | Department | 40h | — |
| Build automated composition test gates (CI/CD pipeline integration, test harness, positive/negative/failure-mode scenarios, and assertions that composed enforcement is ≥ individual component enforcement) | DevOps Lead | Department | 56h | £12,000 |
| Create auditable architecture and distributed interaction analysis pack (communication protocols, retry/redelivery semantics, eventual consistency windows, emergent policy gap assessment, and recorded rationale) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Establish lifecycle procedure and governance controls (spec/design/dev/modify gates, named responsibilities, exception workflow, evidence retention requirements, and release checklist integration) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Run pilot on representative services and remediate gaps (update policy contract, policy-as-code, and composition tests until acceptance criteria are met) | Software Lead | Business Unit | 32h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 248h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £12,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a policy agreement that lists the set of distributed components that implement the same security policy scope (sa-08.17_odp).
- [ ] Put the shared security rules into version-controlled “policy as code” (or equivalent enterprise policy templates), and make sure that calls between services cannot avoid or skip those rules.
- [ ] Define measurable “at least as effectively” requirements for the policy’s scope (for example, the allowed or denied outcomes, the logging and audit events to record, and any encryption requirements).
- [ ] Add continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) “composition” test checkpoints that run integration tests across the identified components, including tests for negative cases and failure scenarios.
- [ ] Review the communication protocols and any methods used to keep distributed data consistent, and record your findings and test evidence for audit.
- [ ] Update the system lifecycle procedure so that any specification, design, development, implementation, or modification of the component set must pass the required composition test checkpoints and must include approved updates to the policy contract.
