#### SA-15.12 — Minimize Personally Identifiable Information (Enhancement)

This requirement is about keeping people’s personal details out of the places where systems are built and tested, so that only the minimum necessary information is ever used. It addresses a real-world risk: development and testing often involve copying real customer or employee information into environments that are less protected, which increases the chance of accidental exposure, misuse, or privacy harm.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the system or component developer to minimise the use of personal data in development and test settings. This should be done using practical steps, such as removing direct identifiers or using artificial data that does not relate to real people. The organisation must also ensure the developer documents how this minimisation is carried out, builds it into day-to-day development and testing, and has clear, approved authority for any rare exceptions where real personal data is used, including keeping evidence of those approvals.

Finally, the organisation must include these expectations in procurement documents and internal plans so they are enforceable from the start.

##### Enforce PII minimisation in dev/test via contractual clauses & CI/CD gates

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Amend procurement/governance artefacts: update acquisition policy/procedures, solicitation language, and contract/SLA/security & privacy plan templates to mandate dev/test PII minimisation (default synthetic/de-identified datasets; exception workflow requirements; evidence retention) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Define acceptable minimisation methods and exception criteria: publish guidance covering de-identification/synthetic data approaches, what constitutes “direct identifiers”, and approval requirements (authoriser, justification, scope, duration, expiry) | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Create dataset manifest standard: specify required manifest fields (schema, PII attributes, transformation/masking/generalisation method, synthetic data approach, pointers to approved source/exception record) and provide a versioning/retention template | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Implement CI/CD and environment provisioning policy-as-code gates: block dev/test dataset imports unless they reference an approved dataset manifest; add automated checks for direct identifiers and enforce “active approved exception reference” requirement | DevOps Lead | Enterprise | 56h | £6,000 |
| Integrate exception workflow with identity/access: ensure only authorised roles can request real PII for dev/test; require pipeline to reference exception ID and validate status/expiry before allowing imports | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Operationalise developer responsibilities and evidence: update engineering playbooks/templates to require a PII minimisation design submission and dataset manifest maintenance per dev/test dataset; define audit-ready evidence pack structure | Project Manager | Enterprise | 18h | — |
| Conduct rollout and assurance: run training/enablement sessions, perform pilot on representative pipelines/datasets, and complete evidence-based gap assessment against acceptance criteria | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 20h | £2,500 |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 178h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £8,500

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update procurement templates (request for proposals, contract, service level agreement (SLA), and security and privacy plan input requirements) to require development and testing teams to minimise personal data by using de-identification and/or synthetic data.
- [ ] Define and publish an exception process for using real personal data (PII) in development and testing, including who can approve it, how long approvals last, and what proof must be kept and for how long.
- [ ] Create a standard dataset “manifest” format that requires identifying any personal data (PII) fields and stating the data minimisation method used for each development and testing (dev/test) dataset.
- [ ] Set up continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) and use “policy as code” checks to stop development and testing environment data imports unless they use an approved dataset manifest.
- [ ] Add automated pipeline checks to detect direct identifiers in development and testing datasets, and stop (fail) the build unless an active exception ID is provided.
- [ ] Require developers to submit a design showing how they will minimise personal data (PII) and a written explanation of the privacy risks, as part of the system security and privacy plan documentation.


---

#### SA-15.13 — Logging Syntax (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that, when software is being built and tested, the logs record security-relevant activity in a clear and consistent way, without accidentally capturing personal information about people. If developers include names, email addresses, or other identifying details in test and development logs, that information can spread beyond its intended use and become harder to protect. It can also make incident investigations slower and less reliable.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set clear rules for suppliers and internal developers so that development and test logs are written in a consistent, structured format. Each event must be recorded as one line, with dates and times in a standard format, consistent field names, and only the minimum information needed: a pseudonymised identifier, whether the event succeeded or failed, the time, where it came from, and a reference that links related events.

The organisation must also ensure these logging rules are included in procurement and contract documents and reflected in the system security plan. The organisation must then check that developers’ test logs follow the required structure and do not include free-text personal details for authentication, authorisation, access to sensitive data, changes to security settings, and access to audit logs.

##### CI/CD-enforced JSONL secure logging schema with PII linting

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define versioned JSONL secure logging schema (v1) including required fields, event type taxonomy, ISO 8601 timestamp rules, and PII minimisation rules for the specified event types | Security Engineering / AppSec | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Implement schema validation library/module for development/test that enforces JSONL (one JSON object per line), required keys, consistent field names, ISO 8601 parsing, and correlationId presence | Software Lead | Department | 40h | — |
| Implement PII-aware linter with deterministic rules (email/phone/address-like detection, structured-field allow/deny logic, and disallowed free-text patterns) and produce rule-failure locations for CI reporting | Security Engineer | Department | 32h | — |
| Integrate CI/CD policy-as-code gates to run schema validation + PII linting on emitted logs during test builds; ensure builds fail on violations and publish machine-readable + human-readable reports | DevOps Lead | Department | 28h | £6,000 |
| Add correlationId propagation validation in development/test (request context/tracing integration, test harness checks, and enforcement that correlationId is emitted for required security event types) | Platform Engineer | Department | 20h | — |
| Implement evidence generation and retention for audit readiness (store schema version, validation results, PII lint findings, and build artefacts with traceable build identifiers) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Update developer guidance and reference implementations (SDK/helper usage patterns, example event payloads, and “how to fix” CI failures) | Software Lead | Business Unit | 16h | — |

Total Estimated Effort: **166h**  
Total Estimated Cost: **£6,000**

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a versioned **JSON Lines** logging format for development and testing that uses **ISO 8601** date and time stamps, consistent field names, a defined set of required event types, and the minimum required fields.
- [ ] Set up continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) checks to confirm that each security event is correctly formatted. These checks should verify the JSON Lines (JSONL) structure, that all timestamps use the ISO 8601 format, that all required fields are present, and that field names stay consistent.
- [ ] Develop and integrate a personal data (PII) checker that prevents free-text or message fields from containing prohibited personal data patterns, and that requires use of pseudonymised identifiers.
- [ ] Add generation and onward passing of a correlation identifier (correlationId) in the application, and check that a correlationId is present in every required security event type.
- [ ] Run automated test suites that generate security event records and save continuous integration (CI) test results (including the schema version, pass/fail outcome, and any personal data (PII) checks) as audit evidence.


---

### SA-16 — Developer-provided Training (Control)

This requirement is about making sure the people who build your system are trained every year to use and run the safety and privacy features they have built in, and to write code in a way that reduces weaknesses. Without this, even well-meaning developers can misunderstand how the built-in protections are supposed to work, implement them incorrectly by accident, or leave gaps that could lead to data being exposed, privacy promises being broken, or security weaknesses being introduced.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must require the system or service developer—whether they are part of your organisation or a third party—to provide annual training. This training must include both online learning and practical, hands-on sessions. It must cover the security and privacy features, secure coding practices, and how to operate and check that the protections work correctly in day-to-day use. You must also keep training materials and records showing who attended and that the training was completed within the last 12 months. Finally, this must be written into procurement and contract documents so it can be enforced.

##### Annual developer security/privacy training as contract deliverable

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Draft and approve enforceable contract/SOW clause requiring annual developer security/privacy training (web-based + hands-on) and audit-ready evidence submission | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Define training scope and learning objectives mapped to the system’s implemented security/privacy functions/controls/mechanisms (including secure coding and correct operation/verification) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Create training outline, module structure, and hands-on exercise plan using a segregated test environment (with prerequisites, success criteria, and verification steps) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Develop training materials (slides/handouts) and secure coding content tailored to the application stack and control mechanisms | Software Lead | Department | 32h | — |
| Implement evidence pack template and evidence collection workflow (attendance/completion records with named individuals, dates, agenda, mapping matrix, and retention approach) | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Deliver first annual training cycle (web-based modules + hands-on session) and produce the audit-ready evidence pack for submission | Project Manager | Business Unit | 24h | £6,000 |
| For in-house developers, run internal governance process to deliver the same annual requirement and generate the same evidence pack format | Operations Lead | Department | 12h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 144h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £6,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update procurement templates (statement of work (SOW), contract, and service level agreement (SLA)) to require annual training for developers on how to correctly use and operate the security and privacy functions, controls, and mechanisms that have been implemented.
- [ ] Define clear training learning objectives and create a mapping matrix that links each objective to the system’s security and privacy controls and the ways they are implemented.
- [ ] Specify delivery requirements for web-based modules, plus hands-on practical training in a separate test or laboratory environment.
- [ ] Provide an evidence pack for each yearly cycle, including named records of attendance and completion, the training materials, and a clear link between each objective and the control it supports.
- [ ] Apply the same annual training and evidence pack process to internal developers through engineering governance.
- [ ] Create an audit-ready record in the repository that links each annual training evidence pack to the relevant system security and privacy documentation.


---

### SA-17 — Developer Security and Privacy Architecture and Design (Control)

This requirement is about making sure the people building your system plan protection and privacy properly before they write the code, rather than treating it as an afterthought. Without a clear, complete design, important safety and privacy measures can be missed, repeated, or added in ways that do not work well together. This can create gaps that may lead to unauthorised access, misuse of personal information, or inconsistent handling of data across the organisation.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the system, component, or service developer to produce a detailed design document and a security and privacy blueprint. These must align with the organisation’s own security and privacy plans, which form part of the organisation’s wider business architecture.

The developer must fully explain what security and privacy measures are needed, where they apply across both the physical parts and the software parts, and how all parts work together as one coordinated approach to provide the required protection.

##### Architecture pack deliverables for security & privacy design (SA-17)

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---|---:|---:|
| Publish/refresh enterprise reference architectures for security and privacy (identity/trust boundaries, data classification handling, encryption/key management expectations, logging/monitoring expectations, privacy requirements incl. consent/retention/erasure where applicable) | Cyber Security Lead | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Create and publish the SA-17 “Architecture Pack” template (mandatory sections, required diagrams/views, security control allocation matrix, privacy control allocation matrix, unified integration narrative, traceability requirements, component + deployment views) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Define SA-17 acceptance criteria and review-gate workflow for internal design gates and external procurement/contracting (including remediation plan triggers and re-review requirements) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Update governance artefacts and tooling approach for traceability (e.g., required identifiers, mapping rules from enterprise requirements → developer mechanisms → components → verification evidence; submission checklist) | Project Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Establish architecture review gates and sign-off process (security architect + privacy/data protection lead + solution/application architect; documented sign-off/remediation and re-review before build/test sign-off) | Security Architecture Lead | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Implement automated completeness/consistency checks for submitted Architecture Packs (section presence, identifier consistency across matrices, data-flow diagram references to data categories; define rules and runbook) | Platform Engineer | Enterprise | 40h | £6,000 |
| Run pilot reviews for a representative set of systems and refine template/criteria based on findings (lessons learned, gap patterns, guidance updates) | Service Delivery Manager | Business Unit | 16h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 172h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £6,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Publish the organisation’s enterprise security architecture and enterprise privacy architecture reference models so developers can align their work.
- [ ] Create and keep an “Architecture Pack” template that includes required sections covering the security and privacy design, the data flows, and a single, consistent explanation of how all systems integrate.
- [ ] Update procurement and contract templates so that an “Architecture Pack” must be submitted and the acceptance criteria for SA-17 must be met before authorisation to start building and testing.
- [ ] Require developers to provide security and privacy responsibility maps for both physical and logical parts of the system, with clear links to the planned proof (verification evidence) that will be used to check those controls.
- [ ] Set up formal architecture review checkpoints involving the security architect, the privacy and data protection lead, and the solution architect, and require a remediation plan to address any identified gaps.
- [ ] Set up automated checks to make sure Architecture Pack submissions are complete and consistent, including that all required sections are included, identifiers match correctly, and the data flow and data categories are aligned.

#### SA-17.1 — Formal Policy Model (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that, before a system is built, the people developing it can clearly and verifiably explain exactly how it will protect sensitive information and personal data. Without this, organisations may end up with software that only claims to provide protection in general terms, but in practice: allows unauthorised access, does not record important events, sends or stores data without proper protection, mishandles highly sensitive information, or keeps and processes personal data in ways that breach legal and privacy obligations.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the system developer, as part of the development work, to produce a formal, documented model that sets out the rules for: access control, audit logging, encryption for data sent over networks and stored on systems, and handling of Confidential and Secret data. It must also set out the rules for lawful processing, data minimisation, retention limits, and breach notification.

The developer must provide evidence that these models are internally consistent and sufficient to ensure the organisation’s security and privacy requirements are correctly applied when the system is implemented. The organisation must include this as a contractual development deliverable.

##### Formal policy models for access, audit, crypto, and data handling

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define modelling scope, policy elements, and in-scope/out-of-scope boundaries per system component/service (authorisation, audit events, crypto enforcement, Confidential/Secret handling; lawful processing, minimisation, retention, breach notification) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Select formal modelling language/approach and document semantics, assumptions, and verification boundaries; confirm suitability for access-control, event/data constraints, crypto/data-lifecycle rules, and privacy predicates | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Produce formal security policy model (nondiscretionary RBAC/ABAC with deny-by-default; audit logging policy with required fields/conditions; encryption-in-transit/at-rest constraints; prohibited data flows for Confidential/Secret) | Security Engineer | Department | 40h | — |
| Produce formal privacy policy model (lawful processing predicates; data minimisation constraints; retention limits; breach notification triggers) | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Perform formal verification for internal consistency and scoped sufficiency; generate verification reports (e.g., contradiction checks, coverage of required predicates/rules, and mapping to enforcement points) | Software Lead | Department | 40h | — |
| Build traceability matrix linking each formal rule/predicate to implementation enforcement points and corresponding test evidence (authorisation middleware, audit pipeline, crypto configuration, data lifecycle controls, breach workflow triggers) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Package contractual artefacts for acceptance: formal model source, verification evidence, traceability matrix, and change-control-ready documentation (versioning, assumptions, limitations) | Project Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 192h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the in-scope security and privacy expectations for each component or service, including: authorisation, audit records, use of encryption, handling of confidential and secret information, lawful processing, data minimisation, data retention, and what events trigger a breach response.
- [ ] Choose and document the formal modelling language or approach and explain what it means (its semantics), including clear boundaries for what is included in scope versus what is excluded out of scope.
- [ ] Create a formal security policy model that covers: role-based access control (RBAC) and attribute-based access control (ABAC), rules for recording and reviewing audit logs, encryption of data while it is being sent and while it is stored, and handling rules for Confidential and Secret data.
- [ ] Create a formal privacy policy template that covers lawful processing, collecting only the minimum necessary data, setting limits on how long data is kept, and defining when and how to notify people in the event of a data breach.
- [ ] Carry out formal verification to prove the system is internally consistent and sufficient for the intended enforcement scope, and keep the verification reports and logs.
- [ ] Create a traceability matrix that links each formal rule or condition to where it will be enforced in the system and what proof will be provided through testing. Include this matrix and the related test evidence as contractual acceptance deliverables.


---

#### SA-17.2 — Security-relevant Components (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people building or supplying a system clearly state exactly which parts are trusted to keep the security protections working. If there is not a complete, agreed list of the hardware, software, and firmware that affect security, important security functions could be missed. This could create weaknesses that attackers could exploit, or it could cause security protections to stop working after changes.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the developer to provide:
- a written definition of the security-relevant hardware
- a written definition of the security-relevant software
- a written definition of the security-relevant firmware

The developer must also provide a written explanation showing why these lists are complete and cover everything the organisation considers trusted for security.

The organisation should include these requirements in procurement and contract documents, so these documents are delivered as part of delivery and handover.

##### Supplier deliverables for security-relevant hardware/software/firmware lists

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---|---:|---:|
| Draft standard procurement/contract clause pack for SA-17(2) handover deliverables (hardware list, software list, firmware list, completeness rationale) including non-acceptance and sign-off gates | Procurement/Commercial owner | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Create supplier-facing template for the four required deliverables (item identifier/version/scope, security property/function mapping, evidence pointer requirements) and define required granularity for multi-tier/microservices boundaries | Security Assurance lead | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Define internal acceptance criteria and review checklist (document completeness, internal consistency, version/build match to release artefacts, environment boundary/scoping for customer-managed vs provider-managed) | Security Assurance lead | Enterprise | 12h | — |
| Establish submission and evidence handling workflow (intake route to security/compliance portal or mailbox, required metadata, retention of acceptance evidence, version control for received documents) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 10h | — |
| Perform initial supplier onboarding review: run the template against 1–2 representative supplier submissions, refine template/checklist based on gaps and ambiguities, and confirm audit-ready acceptance evidence format | Security Assurance lead | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Train procurement and contract managers on the new clauses and acceptance gates; publish guidance for how to request/validate the deliverables during procurement and at handover | Project Manager | Enterprise | 12h | — |
| Update governance artefacts: add SA-17(2) deliverable requirements to the enterprise security assurance/third-party onboarding standard operating procedure and maintain a reusable clause/template library | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 14h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 108h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Draft and get approval for standard procurement and contract clauses that require SA-17(2) deliverables, including lists of required hardware, software, and firmware, plus a justification explaining why the list is complete.
- [ ] Publish a developer template that requires, for each item, a security-function mapping and links to evidence in the architecture, bill of materials (BOM), software, and firmware inventories.
- [ ] Set up acceptance checks (“acceptance gates”) that prevent handover until all four documents are provided and they all match each other internally, including the document version/build and which environment they apply to.
- [ ] Require suppliers to include an environment boundary statement for cloud software as a service (SaaS) (whether it is managed by the customer or by the provider) within the completeness rationale.
- [ ] Set up a review process for application security and security assurance to check that all required items have been mapped completely, and to keep evidence showing what was accepted.


---

#### SA-17.3 — Formal Correspondence (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people building your system clearly and formally document how the security-related parts connect to the underlying hardware and software, including what happens when something goes wrong. Without this, there is a risk that security protections may work differently in real use than intended, which could lead to unauthorised access, data exposure, or failures that are difficult to spot and explain.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the system or service developer, as part of development, to produce and keep a formal top-level description of every security-relevant interface. This description must state the abnormal situations (exceptions), the exact error information produced, and the resulting outcomes (effects). The developer must also provide evidence that this description matches the organisation’s security rules to the extent that can be shown rigorously: that it covers all relevant interfaces and that it accurately reflects what is actually implemented.

Finally, the developer must document any security-relevant internal mechanisms that exist but are not covered in the formal description.

##### Formal interface correspondence pack for security-relevant APIs

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Create security-relevant interface inventory (scope, interface list, operation catalogue, ownership, environment coverage, and change triggers) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Produce formal top-level interface specifications for each in-scope interface (inputs/outputs, first-class exceptions, error code/message mapping, and effects/state changes) | Software Lead | Enterprise | 56h | — |
| Build policy-model correspondence mapping (link policy invariants → interface operations/effects; define proof strategy; run formal proof where feasible; document structured informal demonstrations where not) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 48h | — |
| Create spec-to-implementation correspondence record (trace each operation to implementing modules/services; compile verification evidence: contract tests, unit/integration tests, property-based tests, static analysis/traceability; document equivalence for middleware/retries/caching/circuit breakers) | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 48h | — |
| Document strictly-internal security mechanism register (internal mapping tables/registers, buffer/memory handling equivalents, token/session storage internals; constraints; evidence that mechanisms cannot violate modeled behaviours) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Establish pack versioning and release/change traceability (version scheme, document control, linkage to release records, audit-ready indexing, and update workflow) | Project Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Run evidence review and gap remediation (audit readiness review, completeness checks against acceptance criteria, corrective actions, and sign-off pack finalisation) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |

Total Estimated Effort: **248h**  
Total Estimated Cost: **£0**

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an inventory of all application interfaces that are relevant to security, including application programming interfaces (APIs), service operations, and internal calls that are critical to security.
- [ ] Create a formal, high-level interface agreement for each interface. Include any exceptions, the error codes and messages, and what changes each interface makes to system state.
- [ ] Match each policy rule (in the formal policy model) to the matching interface actions and their outcomes.
- [ ] Where possible, provide evidence reports. Where evidence cannot be produced in a practical way, run structured informal demonstrations to show that the policy is consistent.
- [ ] Create and keep a record that links each interface operation to the relevant code modules and the proof used to verify that it works.
- [ ] Document any strictly internal security measures that are not covered by the main formal interface, and record the constraints and evidence showing that they cannot change the behaviours described in the model.
- [ ] Create a version history for the Formal Correspondence Pack and link each version to the relevant release or change records so it can be audited.


---

#### SA-17.4 — Informal Correspondence (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people building the system clearly explain, in plain language, how the system’s security-critical parts communicate with each other and what happens when something goes wrong. Without this, the system could behave differently from what the organisation intends—allowing unauthorised actions, concealing failures, or creating confusing results that make problems harder to spot and fix.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the system developer to produce, during development, an informal high-level description of every security-relevant interface. For each interface, the developer must state:
- what exceptions can occur,
- the exact error messages that users or other systems will see, and
- the observable effects on the system.

The developer must also show that this description matches the organisation’s formal security rules, confirm that nothing security-relevant is missing or described incorrectly, and separately document any security-relevant mechanisms that are internal only and not covered by the interface description.

##### Informal security interface behaviour spec with policy alignment evidence

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Produce complete security-relevant interface inventory (including web/API endpoints, admin/privileged operations, inter-service calls, message-bus topics/handlers, device management endpoints where applicable) with interface identifiers and security relevance rationale | Project Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Create “Security-relevant Interface Behaviour” informal specification template and populate it for every interface (exceptions, exact error messages/codes, observable effects; inputs/outputs relevant to security decisions) | Software Lead | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Build traceability matrix demonstrating complete coverage from interface inventory → specification sections (and flag any gaps/assumptions for sign-off) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Assemble informal accuracy evidence pack: run representative failure-case tests (contract/integration/staging) capturing exact error payloads/statuses/codes and confirming observable effects (e.g., no state change, audit/log emission, session not created, transaction rollback) | QA/test lead | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Provide informal policy-model alignment narrative pack: map each interface exception/effect to relevant formal policy model elements using sa-17.04_odp approach; include justification for behavioural correspondence (what is transformed, what is not) | Security Architect | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Document “Strictly internal security mechanisms (not covered by interface behaviour)” in a separate section (plain language; internal-only mapping registers, direct memory I/O, internal secure decision logic; explicitly state not caller-observable) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Conduct assurance review and sign-off workshop (security architect + QA + project owner) to validate completeness, accuracy evidence sufficiency, and consistency with sa-17.04_odp; record outcomes and remediation actions | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 12h | — |

Total Estimated Effort: **156h**  
Total Estimated Cost: **£0**

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep an up-to-date complete list of all system, component, or service connection points that are relevant to security.
- [ ] Create a standard template for security-relevant behaviour of interfaces, including any exceptions, the exact error messages and error codes, and the observable effects.
- [ ] Complete the template for every interface, and link each entry to the relevant parts of the formal policy model using **sa-17.04_odp**.
- [ ] Create an informal coverage traceability matrix to show that no security-relevant interface is missing or listed more than once.
- [ ] Collect an evidence pack from representative failure tests and staging runs that show the actual error messages (error payloads) and the observed outcomes match the specification.
- [ ] Document internal security controls in plain language, separately from any caller-facing behaviour, and make clear that these controls are not observable through the caller’s interface.


---

#### SA-17.5 — Conceptually Simple Design (Enhancement)

This requirement is about building security in a way that is easy to understand and difficult to do incorrectly. The system’s most important security functions should all use one complete, straightforward protection approach. The rules and expected results should have a clear, unambiguous meaning, and the internal design should be set up specifically to deliver that approach.  

If this is not done, security features can become mixed together, work in conflicting ways, or only be partly effective. This can leave weaknesses that attackers can exploit and makes it harder for the organisation to show that the protection works as intended.  

To meet this expectation, the organisation must ensure the system developer:  
- identifies which hardware, software, and firmware parts affect security  
- designs and structures those parts around a single simple protection mechanism  
- defines and documents exactly how the mechanism behaves from start to finish  
- shows how each internal component maps to the mechanism it implements  

The developer must also provide design and development documentation so others can check the approach and confirm there are no other competing or overlapping protection mechanisms.

##### Single conceptually simple security mechanism for all security decisions

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define the single authoritative protection mechanism contract (inputs, minimal state model, decision outcomes, explicit failure modes incl. fail-closed vs fail-open) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Perform security-relevant call-path discovery and gap analysis across presentation, APIs, admin functions, background jobs, and any security agents/firmware (where applicable) to identify all enforcement points | Cyber Security Lead | Enterprise | 48h | — |
| Select and implement the central enforcement component (e.g., authorisation decision point / policy engine middleware) with a normalised request context and thin enforcement interfaces | Software Lead | Enterprise | 120h | £25,000 |
| Refactor application services/endpoints to route all security-relevant decisions through the mechanism (remove/disable duplicated policy logic and replace with enforcement calls) | Platform Engineer | Enterprise | 160h | — |
| Implement audit/event generation strictly from mechanism outputs (consistent decision IDs, reason codes, and failure-mode logging) and integrate with existing SIEM/observability pipelines | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 56h | £6,000 |
| Produce module-to-mechanism mapping evidence (complete inventory of components → mechanism elements implemented: decision logic, input normalisation, enforcement hooks, audit generation) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Develop and execute test suite proving single-mechanism behaviour and absence of competing logic, including failure-mode tests and cross-endpoint consistency checks; produce test evidence pack | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 72h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 528h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £31,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify all parts of the system and all key decision points across the full technology stack where security checks are currently enforced.
- [ ] Define one protection mechanism contract, including its inputs, how its state works, what it outputs, and the specific ways it can fail. Publish this contract in the system design documentation.
- [ ] Refactor security-related components so they all use one single decision-making mechanism for security and remove duplicated or conflicting policy logic.
- [ ] Set up lightweight control layers that apply the decision made by the existing mechanism, without rewriting or re-encoding the rules.
- [ ] Create and keep up to date a record that links each internal component to the mechanism elements it implements.
- [ ] Add tests (including failure scenarios) to show that the behaviour is consistent and that there are no other ways to enforce the requirement.
- [ ] Produce developer documentation and architecture evidence showing that the design is conceptually simple and that security analysis can be traced back to the design decisions.


---

#### SA-17.6 — Structure for Testing (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people who design your system build the important security parts in a way that can be tested properly, consistently, and in the same way each time. If the design is difficult to review, or if key details are missing, security weaknesses may not be found until it is too late—leaving the organisation exposed to data loss, fraud, or service disruption.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require—through procurement and internal planning documents—that developers clearly state which hardware, software, and firmware parts affect security, structure the design so testing can be carried out thoroughly, and include the necessary design and configuration documentation as part of delivery. The organisation should also ensure that its own system design and security planning documents use this same test-friendly structure, so testers can link each security feature to the specific components and settings they need to check.

##### Test-structured security design deliverables in dev contracts

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Draft and approve a new contractual schedule: “Security Test-Structure Deliverables” including scope, required artefacts, delivery format, and acceptance/remediation triggers | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Define the security-relevant scope and minimum coverage requirements (security functions, components/interfaces/configuration points, trust boundaries) with enterprise-context examples (authn/authz enforcement, crypto/key-management integration, secure boot/attestation where applicable, audit/logging pipelines, security configuration parameters) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Specify the required “structured design mapping” template and evidence pack structure (security function → components → interfaces/configuration parameters → trust boundaries) including guidance for traceability and tester usability | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Define module/component inventory requirements for handover (e.g., SBOM/module inventory at appropriate granularity) and how security-relevant components must be identified and linked to the structured design mapping | Data Protection Officer | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Update contract acceptance criteria and release governance workflow so missing/incomplete evidence results in non-acceptance or remediation before test/production sign-off | Project Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Implement delivery-pipeline automated checks (where feasible) for presence/validity of required artefacts (structured mapping, interface/configuration documentation, SBOM/module manifests, test-readiness notes) and integrate results into release gates | DevOps Lead | Enterprise | 40h | £6,000 |
| Run a pilot with one representative developer delivery: validate that testers can plan and execute repeatable security tests using the evidence pack; capture gaps and refine templates/requirements | Quality Assurance Lead | Business Unit | 24h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 176h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £6,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update procurement and software development contract templates to include a mandatory “Security Test-Structure Deliverables” schedule.
- [ ] Define and publish the parts of the organisation’s application that are relevant to security—specifically, the security functions and the application components, interfaces, and configuration points that carry them out.
- [ ] Require developers to provide a clear, traceable record showing how each security function maps to the relevant system components, interfaces, configuration settings, and trust boundaries.
- [ ] Require delivery of test setup design documents and configuration documents that are detailed enough to recreate security-relevant behaviour in test environments.
- [ ] Provide evidence of a complete inventory of software modules and components (for example, a software bill of materials (SBOM) or an equivalent document) at a level of detail appropriate for security-relevant elements.
- [ ] Set clear acceptance criteria so that any missing or incomplete test setup items block a release to testing or production, and trigger fixes.
- [ ] Set up checks in the delivery pipeline to confirm that all required items are included: the mapping, interface specifications, configuration test-readiness notes, and the inventory.


---

#### SA-17.7 — Structure for Least Privilege (Enhancement)

This requirement is about designing systems so that each part only has the minimum access it needs to do its job. The risk it addresses is that if software, firmware, or hardware components are given too much access, a mistake, misuse, or failure in one part could cause more damage than necessary. It also becomes harder to understand and prove what the system can do.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must, when commissioning or developing systems, instruct the developer to:
- identify which parts are security-relevant, and
- design and document how those parts are set up to support limited access.

The organisation should require clear descriptions of the internal design, including:
- how different roles can perform different actions, and
- the minimum access each module has,
including how functions related to auditing are separated by role.

The organisation must also ensure these “least privilege” requirements are included in procurement documents, and then referenced in the system’s security plan, supported by design, architecture, and configuration evidence.

- Priority: Critical

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update procurement and statement of work (SoW) templates to require least-privilege structuring deliverables under security requirement SA-17(7) for security-relevant hardware, software, and firmware.
- [ ] Define and publish the required document templates for the following items: the Security-Relevant Elements and Privilege Model, the Role-to-Interface and Capability Matrix, and the Module Decomposition and Encapsulation Design.
- [ ] Require the developer to show, for the release baseline, a clear trail from the least-privilege requirements through the design and to the implemented configuration evidence.
- [ ] Set up design review “acceptance gates” to check that users are given the minimum necessary permissions, that permissions are broken down into fine-grained parts, and that interfaces only allow actions that match each user’s role.
- [ ] Set up release approval checkpoints that check the configuration evidence (for example, granted access permissions and their scopes, plus the relevant firmware and software settings) matches the documented access and privilege rules.
- [ ] Document and get approval for any justified exceptions to least privilege, with sign-off from the security authority and a recorded reason.


---

#### SA-17.8 — Orchestration (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure the most important systems that control who can access your organisation and how encryption keys are managed are updated in a coordinated, predictable way, rather than one-off changes. Without this, a patch or change made on one system could be assumed to be in place on another system that has not yet been updated. This can lead to inconsistent protection, security rules being applied differently across systems, or knock-on problems where one issue causes others.

To meet this, the organisation must design its mission-critical authentication, access control, and key management services for Secret and Classified information so that security updates and changes follow a defined order across all critical systems. First, patches must be applied and checked. Then, any related configuration changes that depend on those patches must be made together. Finally, the same protection rules must be enforced across all systems. The organisation must document this coordinated approach, reflect it in its security and privacy planning, and require it through architecture and procurement documents, including service agreements and contracts, with named responsibilities for those who design, buy, and oversee the overall co-ordination.

##### Orchestrated patch-and-policy release gates for critical auth/KMS

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define orchestration scope, in-scope critical systems list, and dependency graph (patch-before-dependent configuration/policy prerequisites) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Design the “single source of policy intent” model (policy-as-code/versioned artefacts, unique policy version identifier, mapping to enforcement points) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Implement orchestration pipeline with stop-go gates (Stage A patch rollout, Stage B verification, Stage C dependent changes/policy promotion) | Platform Engineer | Enterprise | 80h | £18,000 |
| Build verification/attestation mechanisms (agent/telemetry integration, patch level reporting, policy version enforcement state checks, evidence capture for audit) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 56h | £6,000 |
| Implement drift detection and remediation workflows (scheduled checks, alerting, automatic rollback/abort triggers, exception handling) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 40h | £4,000 |
| Integrate orchestration gates into change/release management workflows (approval hooks, release criteria, audit log retention, rollback runbooks) | Project Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Produce governance artefacts and compliance evidence pack (orchestration design doc, responsibilities/RACI, control traceability to SA-17(8), test results, operational procedures) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 28h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 284h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £28,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define what is included in the orchestration scope for mission-critical services that support authentication, access control, and key management for Secret and Classified data.
- [ ] Create a dependency map showing which configuration or policy changes depend on which required patch levels and prerequisites across all in-scope critical systems.
- [ ] Use one shared source of policy intent, with a single unique policy version identifier that every in-scope system uses.
- [ ] Set up an automated process to roll out software updates, check that all in-scope systems have the correct update level, and only then apply any configuration or policy changes that depend on those updates.
- [ ] Add stop/go checkpoints and rollback or abort steps so dependent changes cannot continue if any system fails patch verification.
- [ ] Set up drift detection to check that all in-scope systems are using the correct expected policy version. If any system does not match, automatically start remediation.
- [ ] Document the orchestration plan—what is included (scope), the order of activities (sequencing), the decision checkpoints (gates), what proof will be collected to confirm it worked (verification evidence), how changes will be undone or stopped (rollback/abort), and who is responsible (responsibilities)—and include the required end-to-end process details in the security planning documents and the relevant contract and service level agreement (SLA) documents.


---

#### SA-17.9 — Design Diversity (Enhancement)

This requirement is about avoiding a single “one-size-fits-all” design for the most important parts that protect sign-ins, permissions, and the safeguarding of encryption keys. The risk it addresses is that if these critical components are built in exactly the same way, then one design flaw, coding mistake, or unexpected weakness could affect every system at once. That would make a serious incident more likely and harder to contain.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require that, for mission-critical authentication, access control, and key management components, more than one developer creates different design options. These options must be based on the same agreed requirements, but they can differ as long as each option provides equivalent protection.

The organisation must set this requirement through procurement and contracting processes, include it in requests for proposals and service agreements where relevant, document how the approach will work, record which design options exist and how each one meets the requirements, ensure configuration records reflect the different options, verify that the options genuinely differ, and keep evidence showing that the work was completed.

##### Requirements-locked dual design variants for auth, access, and KMS

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|

| Define and publish stable interface contracts for each scoped component (identity protocol semantics, PDP/PEP decision interface, KMS key usage/rotation/audit event schemas) with conformance mapping | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 48h | — |
| Establish shared conformance test suite (test cases, security regression scenarios, expected outcomes, evidence format) and define pass/fail equivalence rules | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 64h | — |
| Procure/contract independent Variant A and Variant B delivery for each scoped component, embedding design diversity requirements, evidence obligations (design rationale + test results), and acceptance gates into SOW/RFP/SLAs | Procurement/Commercial Lead | Enterprise | 40h | £25,000 |
| Develop Variant A and Variant B implementation plans and design rationale packs demonstrating meaningful design diversity (e.g., different crypto libraries, different authorisation decision logic, different key lifecycle workflows) | Software Lead | Department | 72h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 400h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £25,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define a requirements document pack and a shared set of compliance tests for mission-critical authentication, access control, and key management components.
- [ ] Publish stable interface contracts covering identity and authorisation (including key management system (KMS) integration behaviour) and the format of audit events that all variants must follow.
- [ ] For each scoped component, select and contract at least two independent development teams or suppliers, and require documented design reasons that clearly show meaningful differences between their approaches.
- [ ] Run the shared compliance checks and security regression tests for each version, and keep verifiable records showing that the functionality is equivalent.
- [ ] Update the configuration management records to link each environment to the deployed variant(s), and document how the system chooses or routes traffic to the correct variant.
- [ ] Update the request for proposal (RFP), statement of work (SOW), and service level agreements (SLAs) to require design variety, independent development, and delivery of the design reasoning and proof that the design has been verified.

### SA-20 — Customized Development of Critical Components (Control)

This requirement is about you rebuilding the most sensitive parts of your on-premises systems yourself, or commissioning their redesign, so they are harder for attackers to break into or tamper with. It focuses on the parts that control who can access the system, how secret keys are handled, and the start-up software that helps ensure the device boots using trusted code. Without this, weaknesses in these critical components could let criminals bypass access controls, steal or misuse secrets, or replace trusted software with malicious versions.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must use documented, controlled development processes and supporting measures that make common attack methods less likely to succeed. It must also keep clear written procedures and detailed design and configuration records showing how the work was done.

In addition, the organisation must keep up-to-date plans for managing supplier risk, procurement rules, change tracking, and audit records, as well as a security plan. Named individuals must be responsible for purchasing, oversight, and the custom development work.

- [ ] Create an inventory of SA-20 components for on-premises systems, covering authentication and access control, cryptographic key management, and secure boot and firmware.
- [ ] Create threat models and security requirements for each SA-20 component, then get security approval (sign-off).
- [ ] Set up a controlled software development life cycle (SDLC) process with approval checkpoints, required code reviews, and recorded evidence for the design, implementation, and testing stages.
- [ ] Rebuild or custom-develop the SA-20 components using more secure methods, including stronger access checks (authentication enforcement), tighter control of encryption key creation, storage, use, and retirement (key lifecycle controls), and starting the system only with trusted software (signed and verified boot), with protection against running older, less secure software versions where applicable (anti-rollback).
- [ ] Require signed release files and maintain controlled build and release integrity, including approval of dependencies and vulnerability scanning, with any exceptions clearly documented.
- [ ] Set up configuration management traceability so that every **SA-20** release can be traced back to the original source code, the build configuration, the cryptographic settings, and the test and verification results.

### SA-21 — Developer Screening (Control)

This requirement is about ensuring that only the correct external people are allowed to work on your on-premises classified development environment and the build process for the Department of Defense (DoD) mission system, and that they are properly checked before they are allowed to access it. Without this, the organisation risks unauthorised changes, accidental or deliberate misuse, or the introduction of unreliable work that could harm the mission or expose sensitive information.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- define which external developers are permitted;
- assign each developer specific responsibilities, such as developing, modifying, testing, or deploying approved code changes; and
- grant access only for the specific responsibilities they are approved to perform, with approval from the system owner.

Before access is granted, each developer must be confirmed as a “US person” with the appropriate clearance for Secret/Confidential information, have passed a background check, provide evidence that they meet the International Traffic in Arms Regulations (ITAR) and Export Administration Regulations (EAR) requirements, complete a nationality review, and undergo a review of the company’s ownership and affiliations.

The organisation must also keep an up-to-date list of all authorised developers so it can verify both their access and their screening records.

##### Screening-gated access for external developers to classified dev pipeline

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define duty-to-permission model for classified dev pipeline (Develop/Modify/Test/Deploy) and map each permission to sa-21_odp.02 duties | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Design and implement “Authorised External Developer Register” (system of record) schema/workflows including required fields (identity, vendor, duties, pipeline scope, system-owner approval reference, screening status, last-updated) | Architect | Enterprise | 40h | £8,000 |
| Implement screening evidence verification workflow (case management integrated with IAM/IGA) to capture and validate all sa-21_odp.03 evidence artefact identifiers/dates before marking “Screening Complete” | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 32h | £6,000 |
| Configure IAM/IGA gating logic: disable external developer access by default; enable only when both “Screening Complete” and system-owner approval exist for the specific duties (RBAC/ABAC enforcement) | IAM/IGA Administrator | Enterprise | 48h | £10,000 |
| Implement system-owner approval gate (recorded approval per duties) and integrate approval events into the enablement workflow with audit trail linkage | Project Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 208h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £31,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define role-based access control (RBAC) and attribute-based access control (ABAC) roles or attributes that link each authorised duty (develop, modify, test, deploy) to the permissions for the classified development environment and build pipeline.
- [ ] Create and keep an official external developer register that records: identity, assigned responsibilities, scope of work, a reference to the system owner’s approval, and the current screening status.
- [ ] Implement a screening evidence verification process that must be completed before marking **“Screening Complete”**. This includes confirming **United States person status and clearance**, completing a **background check**, providing an **International Traffic in Arms Regulations (ITAR) / Export Administration Regulations (EAR) attestation**, completing a **nationality review**, and completing a **review of the vendor’s ownership and affiliations**.
- [ ] Set up identity and access management (IAM) and identity governance and administration (IGA) controls so that external developer accounts are disabled by default. Enable them only when the register shows both (1) screening is complete and (2) the system owner has approved the specific duties.
- [ ] Ensure that every access grant can be traced back to the relevant register entry and the associated evidence document identifiers, so it can be verified during audits.
- [ ] Set up offboarding triggers so that when a developer is removed from the register, their duties and access to the classified development environment and build pipeline are revoked.

### SA-22 — Unsupported System Components (Control)

This requirement is about making sure parts of your systems that are no longer supported do not keep running after the people who made them stop providing updates and help. If you continue using components that no longer receive security fixes, you increase the risk that known weaknesses could be used by attackers. This could lead to service disruption, data loss, or fraud.

The organisation must replace any component as soon as the developer, supplier, or manufacturer can no longer support it. This includes replacing it with security updates, firmware updates, replacement parts, or maintenance where applicable.

If a component is so critical that it cannot be replaced, the organisation must document the reason and obtain approval to keep it in use. It must also choose how support will continue: either provide support internally or arrange support from external providers.

The organisation must keep records showing what was replaced, which support option was chosen, and the approvals and evidence that support arrangements and any measures to reduce risk are in place.

- [ ] Update the authoritative inventory and configuration management database (CMDB) to record vendor identifiers, component versions and firmware levels, and support-status information.
- [ ] Set up an automated check that identifies components that have moved past the unsupported trigger and automatically creates replacement work orders.
- [ ] Set up the change and exception process so that continued use is blocked unless the approval record includes a clear reason and explicitly states whether in-house support or support from external providers will be used.
- [ ] Set up evidence capture so every replacement or exception is linked to the relevant inventory snapshot, the change record, and the approval ID, so it can be audited.
- [ ] Link exception records to the system security plan and provide the supply chain risk management documents to ensure governance traceability.

### SA-23 — Specialization (Control)

This requirement is about ensuring that when the organisation improves the systems used to verify who people are, it does so in a controlled way that genuinely makes those systems more reliable and trustworthy. Without this, changes to sign-in and identity services—along with the supporting servers and any identity-sharing components—could be made in an unplanned, ad hoc way. That could introduce weaknesses, errors, or untested vendor add-ons that reduce confidence in who is actually accessing services.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define which types of design changes and add-on components it will allow for these critical identity services. It must then use documented, step-by-step procedures to plan, approve, implement, and check each change. Every change must be intended to improve trustworthiness, and it must apply to both the main identity services and their supporting components. All changes must be fully recorded with supporting evidence in the system security plan, the supply chain risk management plan, and any other relevant records. Responsibility must be clearly assigned and named across procurement, information security oversight, security architecture, and configuration management.

- [ ] Document step-by-step procedures for improving identity design, covering: request intake, approvals, implementation, testing, rollback, and checks after deployment.
- [ ] Set up the change management process so that every identity update within the SA-23 scope requires approval from Information Security and the Security Architecture team.
- [ ] Set agreed “baseline” configuration settings and record before-and-after snapshots of the identity provider (IdP) and federation settings for each approved augmentation.
- [ ] Update the system security plan and, where supplier or vendor components are involved, the supply chain risk management plan. Include references to each update (augmentation) and the evidence supporting it.
- [ ] Assign named responsibilities (acquisition owner, information security oversight, security architecture, and configuration management) and record them in the procedure.

### SA-24 — Design For Cyber Resiliency (Control)

This expectation is about designing your systems so they keep working, limit how far any problem spreads, and recover quickly even if something goes wrong. Without this, an attacker or a serious internal failure could shut down key services, damage important information, cause long outages, and leave the organisation unable to carry out its mission or protect its reputation.

To meet this, the organisation must set clear cyber resilience goals to keep the mission running, limit the “blast radius” (how much is affected), and ensure recovery happens on time. It must also set objectives to reduce the chance of systems being compromised, keep critical services running during an attack, and restore the accuracy and availability of information within agreed timeframes.

The organisation must build in practical protections, such as separating system parts, giving staff and systems only the minimum access they need, using authentication designed to resist common attacks, providing backup capacity so services can continue, and using backups that cannot be changed or tampered with.

These decisions must be applied during design reviews, based on the threats most likely to occur. The organisation must also continuously check that the approach works through testing and exercises. Planning must assume that a breach could happen, systems should fail in a controlled way, trust should be kept to a minimum, operations should be able to run in a reduced mode if needed, and the design should support recovery and rebuilding. These requirements must be carried through in specifications, development, implementation, and changes.

##### Resilient design blueprint with RTO/RPO, blast-radius limits, and reconstitution

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---|---:|---:|
| Draft system-specific cyber resiliency statement (goals, objectives, techniques, design principles) including maintain mission capability, blast-radius limits, and timely recovery | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Define measurable engineering targets per critical service: RTO/RPO, integrity expectations, and “last known-good” verification method (e.g., checksum/signature + application-level consistency checks) | System Design Authority | Business Unit | 24h | — |
| Produce resiliency technique-to-target mapping and update architecture/design artefacts (segmentation/isolation, least privilege, phishing-resistant MFA, redundancy/failover, immutable backups) with explicit design-review rationale | Security Engineer | Department | 32h | — |
| Establish and document the “cyber resiliency engineering” design-review gate and sign-off checklist (evidence requirements, threat-informed rationale, mapping completeness, approval workflow) | Project Manager | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Execute immutable backup restore test with integrity verification and record audit-ready evidence (test plan, run logs, results, remediation actions) | DR/Operations Lead | Team | 24h | £3,000 |
| Execute DR/failover and reconstitution exercise (tabletop + technical as applicable) demonstrating blast-radius containment, graceful degradation, and documented reconstitution steps; capture pass/fail evidence | Service Delivery Manager | Team | 28h | £5,000 |
| Create reconstitution runbooks and operational procedures (roles, triggers, step-by-step recovery, integrity validation gates, communications/handovers) aligned to the defined RTO/RPO | Operations Lead | Department | 20h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 164h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £8,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and get approval for a cyber resilience statement specific to the system. It should set out the goals, objectives, methods, how it will be implemented, and the design principles.
- [ ] Define the recovery time objective (RTO) and recovery point objective (RPO), and the integrity verification requirements, for each critical service. Record these in the resilience records.
- [ ] Update the architecture and design documents to clearly show how each resilience measure—network segmentation, least privilege, secure-by-design authentication, redundancy, and immutable backups—maps to the stated goals and objectives.
- [ ] Add a mandatory cyber resilience engineering checkpoint to design reviews, and require approval of the maximum impact area (blast radius) limits and the steps for restoring services (reconstitution).
- [ ] Run an unchangeable backup restore test, check that the restored data is complete and correct, and keep audit-ready proof of the results.
- [ ] Run a disaster recovery (DR) and failover exercise, plus a tabletop and technical exercise, to confirm that the organisation’s systems degrade gracefully, are contained, and can be restored using the documented procedures.
